Preface - Volume 3: AI in Production

This volume starts after the demo works.

You already know what a transformer is. You have built RAG systems, tool-using assistants, agents, multimodal pipelines, and aligned chat models in the earlier volumes. The question now is different:

Can this system survive real users, real traffic, real money, real failures,
real adversaries, and real governance requirements?

That is production AI.

Production AI is not just deployment. Deployment is one step. Production is the full operating discipline around the model: serving, routing, batching, caching, release gates, observability, incident response, security, privacy, cost control, safety evaluation, red teaming, documentation, and regulatory readiness.

The central claim of this volume is simple:

The model is only one component.
The production system is what users actually experience.

Who This Volume Is For

This volume is for engineers who can already build an AI application and now need to operate one.

You should be comfortable with:

  • Python
  • HTTP APIs
  • basic cloud infrastructure
  • Docker or containers
  • logs and metrics
  • LLM APIs
  • embeddings and retrieval
  • tool calling or agents

You do not need to be a Kubernetes expert, GPU kernel engineer, lawyer, safety researcher, or SRE before starting. The volume explains the parts you need when they matter.

What You Will Build Toward

By the end, you should be able to reason about a production AI system along five axes:

performance:
  latency, throughput, batching, caching, GPU utilization

economics:
  cost per request, cost per token, workload shaping, model routing

operations:
  release engineering, SLOs, observability, incidents, rollback

safety:
  risk assessment, guardrails, evals, red teaming, human oversight

governance:
  documentation, provenance, privacy, regulation, external review

The final chapter turns those axes into capstone projects. Those projects are not optional decoration. They are how the volume proves itself.

How To Read It

Read Chapters 1-4 in order if your main gap is infrastructure. They build from serving mechanics to cost optimization, deployment, SRE, security, compliance, and FinOps.

Read Chapters 5-7 in order if your main gap is safety and governance. They move from responsible deployment to alignment evaluation, red teaming, frontier frameworks, documentation, provenance, privacy, regulation, and dual-use risk.

Read Chapter 8 when you are ready to build. Pick one capstone first. Finish it properly. A single measured project with a clear eval report is worth more than five half-finished demos.

The Standard

For this volume, “done” means more than “it runs.”

Done means:

  • there is a reproducible way to deploy it
  • there is a measured latency and cost profile
  • there are logs and traces
  • there is an eval set
  • there is a rollback path
  • there is a safety story
  • there is a governance story
  • there is a clear statement of what the system should not do

That is the bar production sets. It is higher than a demo, and it should be.

Chapter 1 - Serving LLMs at Scale

Serving a large language model looks simple from the outside.

You send text to an API. Tokens stream back. The product team sees a chat bubble. The user sees an answer. The demo works.

Inside the serving system, nothing is simple.

A single request may touch an API gateway, rate limiter, authentication service, model router, scheduler, GPU worker, key-value cache, streaming transport, observability collector, cost meter, and safety layer before the first token reaches the user. A production service may do that thousands of times per second while keeping latency low, GPU utilization high, memory pressure controlled, and cost predictable.

This is the gap between “I can run a model” and “I can serve a model.”

Training gets the headlines because training looks heroic: giant clusters, weeks of compute, frontier models. Inference is quieter, but over the lifetime of a product it often costs more. Training is a capital event. Serving is a monthly bill. Every user prompt burns tokens. Every generated token reads model weights. Every idle GPU drains money. Every tail-latency spike becomes a support ticket.

This chapter is the first chapter of Volume 3: AI in Production. Volume 2 taught you how modern models, LLM applications, tools, agents, autonomy, and multi-agent systems work. Volume 3 is about the production machinery that lets those systems survive real traffic.

We start with the most important production problem in modern AI:

How do you serve LLMs at scale without destroying latency, reliability, or budget?

By the end of this chapter, you should understand the shape of modern LLM serving:

  • managed LLM platforms such as OpenAI, Azure OpenAI, Vertex AI, and Bedrock
  • inference economics and why token generation is expensive
  • Kubernetes-based GPU autoscaling
  • vLLM internals: PagedAttention, continuous batching, chunked prefill
  • EAGLE-3 speculative decoding
  • SGLang RadixAttention for prefix-heavy workloads
  • TensorRT-LLM on NVIDIA Blackwell
  • disaggregated prefill/decode
  • the vLLM production stack and LMCache

The goal is not to memorize every product name. Products change. The goal is to learn the systems thinking underneath them.

The Shape of an LLM Serving System

Simple Intuition

Think of an LLM serving system as a high-end restaurant.

The user places an order. The front desk checks the reservation. The kitchen decides which station handles the dish. Some ingredients are already prepared. Some need to be cooked fresh. The kitchen tries to batch similar work together, avoid idle staff, and send dishes out quickly without burning quality.

An LLM serving system is similar:

User request
  -> API gateway
  -> authentication and rate limits
  -> model router
  -> inference scheduler
  -> GPU engine
  -> token stream
  -> observability and billing

The “kitchen” is the GPU cluster. The “ingredients” are tokens, model weights, and the key-value cache. The “wait time” is latency. The “number of dishes per hour” is throughput. The “food cost” is cost per token.

Why Serving Is Difficult

LLM serving is difficult because it combines four hard problems:

Problem Why it is hard
latency users expect first tokens quickly
throughput GPUs must stay busy under mixed traffic
memory key-value cache grows with sequence length
cost GPUs are expensive even when idle

The hard part is that these goals fight each other.

If you maximize throughput, you may batch requests aggressively. That improves GPU utilization but increases first-token latency.

If you minimize latency, you may serve requests immediately with small batches. That improves user experience but wastes GPU capacity.

If you allow long contexts, memory pressure rises. That reduces concurrency.

If you keep too much warm capacity, latency improves but idle GPU cost explodes.

Production serving is the art of choosing the right compromise for a workload.

The Two Phases of Generation

Every autoregressive LLM request has two major phases:

prefill -> decode -> decode -> decode -> ...

Prefill processes the input prompt. If the prompt is 8,000 tokens, the model reads all 8,000 tokens and builds internal attention state.

Decode generates the output one token at a time. Each new token depends on all previous tokens.

The phases behave differently:

Phase What happens Bottleneck
prefill process prompt tokens in parallel compute
decode generate one token per sequence per step memory bandwidth

This split explains most of the serving stack. vLLM optimizes memory and batching. EAGLE-3 accelerates decode. SGLang reuses prefix prefill. Disaggregated serving separates prefill and decode onto different GPU pools. LMCache moves key-value cache out of GPU memory when needed.

Key Takeaways

  • Serving is a systems problem, not only a model problem.
  • LLM serving is constrained by latency, throughput, memory, and cost.
  • Prefill and decode behave differently, so production systems optimize them differently.
  • The serving engine and scheduler often matter as much as the model weights.

Common Mistakes

  • Treating inference as “just call the model.”
  • Optimizing average latency while ignoring tail latency.
  • Measuring raw tokens per second without checking user-facing service-level objectives.
  • Forgetting that idle GPUs still cost money.

Production Insights

  • Always track both time to first token and inter-token latency.
  • Separate workload classes: chat, batch, RAG, agents, code generation, voice, and long-context requests behave differently.
  • Cost per successful request is more useful than cost per raw token.

Managed LLM Platforms

Simple Intuition

A managed LLM platform is like renting a professional kitchen instead of building one.

You do not buy ovens, hire maintenance staff, manage gas lines, or schedule cleaning. You pay to use a working kitchen. You lose some control, but you gain speed, reliability, and less operational burden.

Managed LLM platforms do the same for inference. They hide the model-serving infrastructure behind an API.

You send:

{
  "model": "frontier-model",
  "messages": [
    {"role": "user", "content": "Summarize this contract."}
  ]
}

You receive streamed tokens.

Behind that API is a massive serving system: GPU clusters, model replicas, schedulers, routers, safety filters, rate limiters, observability, billing, and abuse monitoring.

What Managed AI Platforms Are

A managed LLM platform provides API-based inference without requiring you to operate the model-serving stack.

Examples include:

Platform Strategy
OpenAI API direct provider API for OpenAI models
Azure OpenAI OpenAI models inside Azure enterprise controls
AWS Bedrock model marketplace across Anthropic, Meta, Amazon, Mistral, Cohere, and others
Google Vertex AI Gemini-first platform with Model Garden for other models

The platform owns:

  • GPU provisioning
  • model loading
  • scaling
  • model upgrades
  • rate limits
  • serving engine internals
  • abuse monitoring
  • enterprise controls
  • billing

You own:

  • prompts
  • product logic
  • retries
  • fallback policy
  • user experience
  • application observability
  • cost governance
  • data policy

That boundary matters. Managed platforms reduce infrastructure work. They do not remove production responsibility.

Why Companies Use Managed Platforms

Companies use managed LLM platforms for five reasons.

First, speed. A team can launch an AI feature in days instead of months.

Second, operational simplicity. You do not need a GPU team on day one.

Third, model access. Frontier models are usually available first through provider APIs.

Fourth, enterprise controls. Azure, AWS, and Google already have identity, logging, network controls, compliance programs, and procurement paths.

Fifth, elasticity. Bursty traffic is easier when the provider absorbs capacity management.

This is why most serious products start managed. Self-hosting only becomes attractive once the workload is large, predictable, latency-sensitive, privacy-sensitive, or strategically important.

API-Based Inference

API-based inference is the simplest serving model:

Application -> Provider API -> Provider serving stack -> Tokens

The application does not know which GPU ran the request. It does not know the batch size. It does not know whether the provider used vLLM, TensorRT-LLM, a custom engine, or specialized hardware.

That abstraction is useful. It is also limiting.

You get:

  • simple integration
  • provider-managed scaling
  • model updates
  • enterprise billing
  • support contracts

You give up:

  • kernel-level optimization
  • exact scheduling control
  • direct GPU utilization metrics
  • custom batching policy
  • full cost visibility
  • full model portability

Infrastructure Abstraction

Infrastructure abstraction is the main value of managed platforms.

If your product uses Bedrock, you do not provision H100 nodes. If your product uses Azure OpenAI, you do not build a vLLM cluster. If your product uses Vertex AI, you do not manage Gemini serving replicas.

The platform hides:

GPU fleet
  -> model replicas
  -> request queues
  -> batching
  -> load balancing
  -> scaling
  -> failures

This lets application teams focus on product behavior.

But abstraction has a cost. When latency spikes, you may see only:

provider latency increased

not:

KV cache pressure rose because long-context traffic shifted to one region

The provider sees the real system. You see the API surface.

Multi-Tenant Serving

Most managed platforms are multi-tenant.

That means many customers share the same serving fleet. The provider isolates requests logically, but capacity is shared.

Multi-tenancy is efficient because not every customer peaks at the same time. It lets the provider run GPUs at high utilization.

It also creates variance:

  • your request may wait behind other customers’ traffic
  • provider rate limits may tighten during regional pressure
  • tail latency can move even when your traffic is unchanged

Dedicated capacity products exist to reduce this variance. Azure OpenAI has Provisioned Throughput Units, often shortened to PTUs. A Provisioned Throughput Unit is reserved inference capacity. AWS Bedrock has Provisioned Throughput. Vertex AI has provisioned capacity for some model families.

The idea is simple:

shared on-demand = lower commitment, more variance
provisioned capacity = fixed commitment, lower variance

Rate Limiting

Rate limiting protects the platform and enforces your contract.

Common limits include:

Limit Meaning
requests per minute how many API calls you can make
tokens per minute input plus output token budget
concurrent requests how many active calls can run
context length maximum prompt plus output length
batch size maximum requests in a batch endpoint

Rate limits are not only provider bureaucracy. They are capacity planning made visible.

If your application hits token-per-minute limits, retries can make the problem worse. A retry storm can turn a temporary throttle into an outage.

Production clients should use:

  • exponential backoff
  • jitter
  • request queues
  • circuit breakers
  • fallback models
  • clear user-facing degradation

Enterprise Considerations

Enterprise buyers care about more than model quality.

They ask:

  • Where is data processed?
  • Is data retained?
  • Can provider staff see prompts?
  • Is there a Business Associate Agreement for healthcare data?
  • Does the platform support private networking?
  • Does it integrate with identity and access management?
  • Are logs exportable?
  • Can spend be attributed by team or product?
  • Can we pin model versions?
  • Can we fail over to another provider?

Managed platforms compete heavily on this surface.

AWS Bedrock fits teams already standardized on AWS Identity and Access Management, Virtual Private Cloud endpoints, CloudWatch, and Bedrock Guardrails.

Azure OpenAI fits enterprises standardized on Microsoft identity, Azure networking, and OpenAI models.

Vertex AI fits Google Cloud teams and products that need Gemini, long context, and multimodal workflows.

Direct OpenAI fits teams that want the fastest access to OpenAI model releases and are comfortable building surrounding enterprise controls themselves or through a gateway.

Security and Compliance

Security for managed inference has several layers:

network security
identity and access control
data retention policy
logging policy
model abuse monitoring
content filtering
audit trails
regional controls

Do not assume “enterprise platform” means your application is compliant. The provider may satisfy its side of a compliance framework while your product mishandles data before or after the API call.

For regulated systems, define:

  • what data may be sent to the model
  • which provider regions are allowed
  • whether prompts and outputs are logged
  • how long logs are retained
  • who can view traces
  • how incidents are reported
  • how model changes are reviewed

Vendor Lock-In

Vendor lock-in is not always bad. Sometimes the provider gives you a unique capability and the tradeoff is worth it.

The mistake is accidental lock-in.

Accidental lock-in happens when:

  • prompts rely on one provider’s hidden behavior
  • tool schemas use provider-specific formats everywhere
  • safety behavior is not tested across models
  • application code assumes one streaming protocol
  • cost attribution depends on one platform’s billing export
  • failover was never tested

For critical systems, use a two-provider minimum policy.

That does not mean every request goes to two providers. It means your architecture can route critical traffic to at least two providers when needed.

Application
  -> AI gateway
      -> OpenAI
      -> Azure OpenAI
      -> Bedrock
      -> Vertex AI

The gateway normalizes authentication, routing, logging, fallback, rate limits, and cost attribution.

Cost vs Control Tradeoff

The central managed-platform tradeoff:

Managed platform Self-hosted platform
faster to launch more control
less ops burden more tuning
provider handles scaling you handle scaling
limited internals visibility full internals visibility
per-token pricing GPU economics
easier enterprise procurement harder infra ownership
vendor dependency model and engine portability

The usual production path is:

  1. Start with managed APIs.
  2. Add an AI gateway for routing, observability, and cost control.
  3. Move high-volume stable workloads to dedicated capacity or self-hosting.
  4. Keep frontier or low-volume workloads managed.

What Happens Internally

A managed platform receives your request, authenticates it, checks quota, classifies it for safety or policy, selects a model deployment, places the request into a scheduler, batches it with compatible requests, runs inference on GPU workers, streams tokens back, records usage, and emits logs or billing records.

The simplified internal flow:

Request
  -> auth
  -> quota/rate limit
  -> safety pre-check
  -> model router
  -> inference scheduler
  -> GPU worker
  -> token streamer
  -> usage meter
  -> logs/billing

You do not see most of this. But when you debug latency, cost, or failures, this hidden flow is what you are debugging through the API surface.

Key Takeaways

  • Managed platforms abstract GPU infrastructure behind APIs.
  • They are the fastest way to ship LLM features.
  • Dedicated capacity reduces variance but introduces fixed cost.
  • Enterprise concerns include identity, data residency, logging, compliance, and cost attribution.
  • Avoid accidental lock-in by putting a gateway and evaluation layer between your product and providers.

Common Mistakes

  • Picking a platform only because one model looks best this month.
  • Ignoring rate limits until launch day.
  • Treating provider logs as safe for sensitive prompts without review.
  • Building no failover path for product-critical calls.
  • Comparing providers only by token price and not by latency, quota, compliance, and observability.

Production Insights

  • Use managed platforms for speed, but instrument them like any dependency.
  • Track provider-level latency, error rate, rate-limit events, and cost per feature.
  • Use dedicated capacity only when utilization is predictable enough to justify fixed spend.
  • Keep a provider abstraction thin. Do not hide model-specific behavior completely, but keep routing and fallback possible.

Inference Platform Economics

Simple Intuition

Inference economics is the business of turning GPU time into tokens without wasting too much money.

Imagine buying a truck for deliveries. If the truck is always full, cost per package is low. If it drives around half-empty, every package is expensive. If it sits parked, you still pay insurance and financing.

GPUs are the truck. Tokens are the packages. Idle GPUs are parked trucks with the engine running.

Why Inference Is Expensive

LLM inference is expensive because every generated token requires the system to touch a very large model.

For a dense 70B parameter model, the weights alone are huge:

Precision Approx bytes per parameter Weight memory
BF16 2 bytes about 140 GB
FP8 1 byte about 70 GB
4-bit 0.5 bytes about 35 GB

BF16 means brain floating point 16, a 16-bit numeric format common in deep learning. FP8 means 8-bit floating point. 4-bit formats compress weights further. Lower precision saves memory and bandwidth, but can reduce quality if done poorly.

During decode, the system generates one token at a time. Each step reads model weights and the key-value cache. The GPU is often limited by memory bandwidth, not pure compute.

That is why decode is expensive:

one token -> read lots of weights -> update attention state -> sample next token

Repeat thousands or millions of times.

GPU Economics

Modern inference GPUs are costly:

  • H100 and H200 are expensive and scarce.
  • B200 and GB200 are more powerful but still expensive.
  • Cloud GPU hourly rates include hardware, power, networking, margin, and scarcity premium.
  • Idle capacity still costs money.

The economic target is high useful utilization.

Useful utilization is not just GPU busy time. A GPU can be 100% busy producing tokens too slowly for your service-level objective. That is bad utilization.

Use goodput instead.

Goodput means throughput that meets the service-level objective.

raw throughput = all tokens per second
goodput = tokens per second from requests that met latency targets

A system producing 10,000 tokens per second with terrible tail latency may have lower goodput than a system producing 7,000 tokens per second reliably.

Token Generation Costs

Cost per token depends on:

  • model size
  • precision
  • hardware
  • batch size
  • context length
  • output length
  • cache reuse
  • utilization
  • provider pricing model

A simple cost model:

cost_per_token = GPU_cost_per_second / tokens_per_second

If one GPU costs $8/hour:

$8 / 3600 seconds = $0.00222 per second

If it produces 2,000 tokens per second:

$0.00222 / 2000 = $0.00000111 per token

That is about $1.11 per million tokens before overhead.

But this is idealized. Real systems add:

  • idle capacity
  • replicas for availability
  • networking
  • CPU workers
  • storage
  • observability
  • engineering time
  • failed requests
  • retries
  • safety filters
  • batch inefficiency

Cost per successful token is higher than raw GPU math.

Throughput vs Latency

Throughput is how much work the system completes per unit time.

Latency is how long one request waits.

Batching improves throughput because GPUs like large matrix operations. But batching can hurt latency because requests wait to be grouped.

The tradeoff:

Strategy Throughput Latency
serve immediately lower lower
wait for batch higher higher first-token delay
continuous batching high controlled
batch API very high not interactive

Interactive chat cares about time to first token and inter-token latency. Batch summarization cares about cost and total completion time.

Do not run both through the same serving policy unless you must.

Batch Sizing

Batch size is not just “number of requests.” In LLM serving, a batch is shaped by tokens.

Two requests are not equal:

Request A: 200 input tokens, 50 output tokens
Request B: 40,000 input tokens, 2,000 output tokens

Batching by request count hides the real load.

Production schedulers think in:

  • total prefill tokens
  • active decode sequences
  • key-value cache blocks
  • maximum batched tokens
  • memory pressure

The right batch size depends on:

  • model size
  • GPU memory
  • context length
  • traffic mix
  • latency objective
  • cache policy

GPU Utilization

GPU utilization metrics are easy to misread.

A common metric from NVIDIA Data Center GPU Manager is DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL. It measures whether the GPU was active during sampling intervals. It is a duty-cycle signal.

That means 100% utilization can mean:

  • healthy high-throughput service
  • overloaded service with huge queue
  • one inefficient request keeping the GPU busy
  • decode-bound workload with poor goodput

For LLM serving, use better signals:

  • queue depth
  • time to first token
  • inter-token latency
  • key-value cache utilization
  • goodput
  • request success rate
  • tokens per GPU-second
  • cost per successful request

Idle GPU Problems

Idle GPUs are one of the fastest ways to burn money.

They happen because:

  • traffic is bursty
  • capacity is over-provisioned for peak
  • model replicas are kept warm
  • autoscaling is slow
  • each team owns separate GPU pools
  • workloads cannot share due to isolation or model mismatch

The painful part is that reducing idle GPU time can hurt latency. If you scale down too aggressively, cold starts become user-visible.

The production compromise is usually:

  • keep minimum warm capacity for critical paths
  • use queue-based autoscaling for bursty workloads
  • push batch traffic to off-peak windows
  • consolidate compatible workloads
  • use lower-cost models for fallback paths

Capacity Planning

Capacity planning asks:

How many GPUs do we need to meet traffic and latency targets?

You need:

  • requests per second
  • input token distribution
  • output token distribution
  • model size
  • target time to first token
  • target inter-token latency
  • expected concurrency
  • peak-to-average traffic ratio
  • failover requirement

A useful planning table:

Metric Example
average requests per second 200
peak requests per second 1,000
P50 input tokens 800
P95 input tokens 8,000
P50 output tokens 200
P95 output tokens 1,500
TTFT target under 800 ms
ITL target under 40 ms
availability target 99.9%

If you only plan from averages, production will hurt you. LLM traffic has fat tails. A few long-context requests can dominate memory and latency.

Why Serving Can Cost More Than Training

Training is expensive once. Serving is expensive forever.

A model may cost tens of millions to train. But if a product serves billions of tokens per day for years, inference cost can exceed training cost.

This is why companies such as OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, Meta, NVIDIA, AWS, and Azure invest deeply in inference optimization. A 10% serving improvement at scale may be worth millions per month.

Real-World Production Economics

Modern inference markets split into several layers:

Segment Examples Best for
managed model APIs OpenAI, Anthropic, Azure OpenAI, Bedrock, Vertex speed and frontier access
GPU inference platforms Fireworks, Together, Baseten, Modal, Anyscale custom models and better economics
API marketplaces Replicate, DeepInfra, OpenRouter broad catalog and quick launches
custom silicon Groq, Cerebras, SambaNova ultra-low latency or specific workloads
self-hosted vLLM, SGLang, TensorRT-LLM maximum control and scale economics

Pricing models differ:

Pricing model Works well when
per token bursty usage, low ops burden
per second serverless GPU functions
per minute high utilization dedicated serving
reserved capacity predictable enterprise load
owned hardware very high sustained usage

Key Takeaways

  • Inference cost is driven by GPU time, memory bandwidth, utilization, and token volume.
  • Cost per token is simple in theory and messy in production.
  • Goodput matters more than raw throughput.
  • Idle GPUs are expensive, but scaling down too aggressively hurts latency.
  • Serving can exceed training cost over the lifetime of a successful product.

Common Mistakes

  • Planning capacity from average request size.
  • Ignoring output token distribution.
  • Treating GPU utilization as the only scaling metric.
  • Comparing vendors without modeling workload shape.
  • Forgetting retries, failures, and safety passes in cost estimates.

Production Insights

  • Track cost per successful request by feature, not only total provider bill.
  • Separate interactive and batch workloads.
  • Watch P95 and P99 token lengths. They drive infrastructure.
  • Use batch APIs or offline queues for non-interactive work.
  • Re-run cost models whenever model, prompt, context length, or output policy changes.

GPU Autoscaling with Kubernetes

Simple Intuition

Kubernetes is a warehouse manager for containers.

You tell it what workloads you want running. It places them on machines, restarts them when they fail, exposes them through services, and scales them when demand changes.

For LLM serving, Kubernetes manages pods that run GPU inference engines.

The hard part: GPUs are not ordinary CPUs. They are expensive, scarce, topology-sensitive, and slow to warm up.

Kubernetes Basics for AI Engineers

A pod is the smallest deployable unit in Kubernetes. For inference, a pod usually contains one model-serving process, such as a vLLM server.

A node is a machine. A GPU node contains one or more GPUs.

A deployment manages replicas of pods.

A service gives pods a stable network endpoint.

A scheduler decides which pod runs on which node.

The simple flow:

Deployment says: run 4 replicas
Kubernetes creates 4 pods
Scheduler places pods on nodes
Service load-balances traffic to pods

For GPUs, pods request GPU resources:

resources:
  limits:
    nvidia.com/gpu: 1

This tells Kubernetes the pod needs one NVIDIA GPU.

GPU Scheduling

GPU scheduling is harder than CPU scheduling because placement matters.

A model may require:

  • one GPU
  • multiple GPUs on one node
  • multiple nodes connected by high-speed networking
  • GPUs connected by NVIDIA NVLink
  • a specific GPU generation such as H100, H200, B200, or GB200

If a model uses tensor parallelism, its layers are split across GPUs. Those GPUs need fast communication. Putting them on random nodes can destroy performance.

Horizontal Pod Autoscaler

The Horizontal Pod Autoscaler, or HPA, scales the number of pods.

Classic HPA scales on CPU:

if CPU > 70%, add pods
if CPU < 30%, remove pods

For LLM serving, CPU is usually the wrong signal. GPU duty cycle is often also the wrong signal.

Better signals:

  • request queue depth
  • time to first token
  • inter-token latency
  • key-value cache utilization
  • active sequences
  • goodput

The scaling question is:

Are users waiting because the serving replicas are saturated?

not:

Is the GPU technically busy?

Cluster Autoscaler and Karpenter

Scaling pods is not enough if there are no nodes to run them.

The Cluster Autoscaler adds or removes nodes from node groups. It works, but GPU nodes can take minutes to appear.

Karpenter is a newer node provisioner that can create nodes dynamically based on pending pods. It is often faster and more flexible for GPU workloads.

The layers:

Application autoscaler adds pods
  -> pods wait because no GPU node exists
  -> Karpenter provisions GPU node
  -> scheduler places pod
  -> model loads
  -> pod becomes ready

The hidden latency is model loading. Even if the node appears in under a minute, loading a large model can take much longer.

GPU Fragmentation

GPU fragmentation means GPUs exist, but not in the shape your workload needs.

Example:

Model needs 8 GPUs on compatible nodes.
Cluster has 7 free GPUs in the right place and 1 free GPU elsewhere.

The scheduler cannot place the workload correctly. Some GPUs may sit idle while the cluster waits for the missing shape.

This is common with large inference jobs that need multi-GPU placement.

Gang Scheduling

Gang scheduling means all-or-nothing scheduling.

If a distributed inference job needs 8 pods, the scheduler starts all 8 together or starts none.

Without gang scheduling, you can get the partial allocation trap:

7 of 8 pods start
1 pod waits
7 GPUs sit idle waiting for the last pod

Tools such as KAI Scheduler handle gang scheduling, topology awareness, and hierarchical queues for GPU clusters.

NVIDIA GPU Operator

The NVIDIA GPU Operator manages NVIDIA GPU software on Kubernetes nodes:

  • drivers
  • NVIDIA container runtime
  • device plugin
  • monitoring components
  • Multi-Instance GPU configuration where supported

Without it, every GPU node becomes a manual systems job. With it, GPU node setup becomes a Kubernetes-managed lifecycle.

Queue-Based Autoscaling

Queue-based autoscaling is often better than utilization-based autoscaling for LLMs.

The signal:

how many requests are waiting?

If the queue grows, add capacity. If the queue drains and stays low, remove capacity.

This maps directly to user experience.

Queue depth is especially useful for prefill-heavy workloads. Key-value cache utilization is useful for decode-heavy workloads.

Scaling During Traffic Spikes

Traffic spikes are dangerous because GPU cold starts are slow.

A spike may require:

  1. more pods
  2. more GPU nodes
  3. model download or load
  4. warmup
  5. readiness probe passing

That can take minutes.

For interactive products, keep warm capacity:

minimum replicas > 0
warm standby nodes
pre-pulled model weights
fast local storage
readiness gates based on model loaded

Scale-to-zero is attractive for cost, but risky for latency-sensitive paths.

Multi-Node Inference

Large models may use multiple GPUs across nodes. Communication becomes part of inference latency.

Common parallelism modes:

Mode Meaning
tensor parallelism split matrix operations across GPUs
pipeline parallelism split layers across GPUs
expert parallelism route mixture-of-experts experts across GPUs

Multi-node inference needs high-speed networking such as InfiniBand or NVLink-based systems. Ordinary Ethernet can become the bottleneck.

Fault Tolerance

GPU nodes fail. Drivers hang. Pods crash. Model servers run out of memory. Network links degrade.

Production systems need:

  • health checks
  • readiness checks
  • request timeouts
  • retry policy
  • load balancer removal for unhealthy pods
  • checkpoint or cache recovery for long requests
  • safe draining before pod termination

Do not kill a pod that is serving active streaming requests unless your client can recover.

Load Balancing

LLM load balancing is not round-robin.

A good load balancer considers:

  • active requests
  • queue depth
  • model loaded
  • key-value cache locality
  • tenant limits
  • context length
  • GPU memory pressure
  • region

For prefix-heavy workloads, cache-aware routing can be decisive. Sending a request to the engine that already has the prefix cached can save a long prefill.

Key Takeaways

  • Kubernetes is useful for AI serving, but GPU workloads need GPU-aware scheduling.
  • Autoscaling has three layers: pods, nodes, and application-level scheduling.
  • GPU utilization is often the wrong autoscaling signal.
  • Queue depth and key-value cache utilization are better serving signals.
  • Gang scheduling prevents partial multi-GPU allocations from wasting GPUs.

Common Mistakes

  • Scaling vLLM pods on CPU usage.
  • Scaling on GPU duty cycle without checking queue depth.
  • Letting Karpenter consolidate running GPU inference nodes aggressively.
  • Ignoring model load time in autoscaling plans.
  • Running multi-GPU workloads without topology-aware scheduling.

Production Insights

  • Keep separate node pools for interactive inference, batch inference, and training.
  • Use taints and tolerations to keep non-GPU workloads off GPU nodes.
  • Pre-pull model weights where possible.
  • Keep minimum warm capacity for user-facing paths.
  • Treat autoscaling as a latency-control system, not just a cost-control system.

vLLM Serving Internals

Simple Intuition

vLLM became popular because it made LLM serving behave less like a wasteful waiting room and more like an efficient operating system.

The old approach reserved large chunks of GPU memory per request and ran batches awkwardly. vLLM manages the key-value cache like virtual memory and schedules requests continuously.

The result: better memory use, higher throughput, and lower tail latency.

What vLLM Is

vLLM is an open-source LLM serving engine focused on high-throughput inference.

It became popular because it combined:

  • PagedAttention
  • continuous batching
  • efficient key-value cache management
  • OpenAI-compatible APIs
  • tensor parallelism support
  • strong integration with production deployment stacks

For many teams, vLLM is the default self-hosted serving engine before moving to more specialized stacks such as SGLang or TensorRT-LLM.

The KV Cache

The key-value cache stores attention keys and values for previous tokens.

Why it exists:

During decode, the model should not recompute attention state for every previous token from scratch. It stores keys and values so the next token can attend to the past efficiently.

The problem:

The key-value cache grows with sequence length and concurrency.

more users -> more active sequences
longer prompts -> more KV cache
longer outputs -> more KV cache

For large models, KV cache memory can become the limiting resource before compute does.

PagedAttention

PagedAttention is vLLM’s key idea.

It treats the key-value cache like virtual memory. Instead of reserving one contiguous block per request, it allocates fixed-size blocks and maps logical token positions to physical memory blocks.

The analogy:

Operating system vLLM
virtual memory pages KV cache blocks
page table block table
process memory sequence cache
free page list free KV block pool

The benefit is lower fragmentation.

Without paging, a request may reserve memory for 8,192 tokens but only use 1,500. That wastes most of the allocation.

With PagedAttention, the request gets blocks as it grows.

logical tokens:
0..15     -> physical block 91
16..31    -> physical block 12
32..47    -> physical block 308

The blocks do not need to be contiguous.

Continuous Batching

Static batching waits for a group of requests, runs them together, and waits until the batch is done.

Continuous batching updates the batch between decode steps.

At each iteration:

  1. remove finished sequences
  2. admit new waiting sequences if memory allows
  3. run one forward pass for the active batch
  4. stream generated tokens
  5. repeat

Diagram:

time 0: [A, B, C]
time 1: [A, B, C, D]   D joins
time 2: [A, C, D]      B finishes
time 3: [A, C, D, E]   E joins

The GPU stays busy with real work instead of waiting for the slowest request in a fixed batch.

Chunked Prefill

Long prompts can block decode.

If one request has a 32k-token prompt, prefill may monopolize the GPU while other users wait for decode tokens.

Chunked prefill splits the prompt into chunks, such as 512 tokens, and interleaves them with decode work.

prefill chunk -> decode step -> prefill chunk -> decode step

This protects inter-token latency for active users.

Chunked prefill mostly improves tail behavior. It is about keeping the system responsive under mixed workloads.

Request Scheduling

The vLLM scheduler has to balance:

  • waiting requests
  • running requests
  • available KV blocks
  • prefill work
  • decode work
  • maximum batched tokens
  • latency objectives

The scheduler is the brain of the serving engine.

Pseudo-flow:

release completed sequences
check available KV blocks
admit new requests
choose prefill chunks
choose decode sequences
run model forward
stream tokens
update KV cache

Token Streaming

Streaming is not only a user-experience feature. It changes perceived latency.

If the first token arrives quickly, users tolerate longer total generation. If nothing appears for several seconds, the product feels broken.

Serving systems track:

  • time to first token
  • inter-token latency
  • total generation time
  • stream interruption rate

vLLM Architecture

A simplified vLLM deployment:

Client
  -> API server
  -> scheduler
  -> model executor
  -> attention kernels
  -> KV cache manager
  -> streamer

In Kubernetes, multiple vLLM engines sit behind a router:

API gateway
  -> router
      -> vLLM engine 1
      -> vLLM engine 2
      -> vLLM engine 3

vLLM vs Hugging Face TGI

Hugging Face Text Generation Inference, or TGI, is another production serving stack.

Dimension vLLM Hugging Face TGI
primary strength throughput and KV cache efficiency Hugging Face ecosystem integration
key mechanism PagedAttention, continuous batching optimized serving for HF models
ecosystem strong self-hosted LLM infra strong model hub integration
common use high-throughput production serving HF-native deployments

TGI is still useful, especially in Hugging Face-centered teams. vLLM became the default for many high-throughput open-source LLM deployments because its scheduler and KV cache design are strong.

Key Takeaways

  • vLLM optimizes the memory and scheduling bottlenecks of LLM serving.
  • PagedAttention reduces KV cache fragmentation.
  • Continuous batching keeps the GPU full as requests arrive and finish.
  • Chunked prefill protects tail latency under long prompts.
  • The scheduler is where much of the serving performance lives.

Common Mistakes

  • Treating vLLM as a black box and tuning random flags.
  • Setting maximum context lengths too high for the real workload.
  • Ignoring KV cache utilization.
  • Mixing long-context and short-chat traffic without isolation.
  • Optimizing throughput while damaging time to first token.

Production Insights

  • Start with vLLM defaults, then measure workload-specific bottlenecks.
  • Watch KV block utilization and preemption events.
  • Use separate serving pools for very long-context traffic.
  • Enable prefix caching only when prompt templates are stable.
  • Benchmark with your real prompt and output distributions.

EAGLE-3 Speculative Decoding

Simple Intuition

Speculative decoding is like having a fast junior engineer draft the next few words, then a senior engineer checks them in one pass.

If the draft is good, the senior engineer approves several words at once. If the draft is bad, the senior corrects it.

The target model is the senior engineer. The draft model or draft head is the junior engineer.

Why Decoding Is Slow

LLMs generate tokens one at a time:

token 1 -> token 2 -> token 3 -> token 4

Each token depends on the previous token. You cannot directly generate token 100 before token 99.

This creates the token-by-token bottleneck.

During decode, the GPU often waits on memory bandwidth because it repeatedly reads model weights to produce one token per sequence.

Speculative Decoding Intuition

Speculative decoding tries to generate multiple tokens per expensive target-model pass.

The process:

  1. A small draft model proposes several tokens.
  2. The large target model verifies those tokens in one forward pass.
  3. Accepted tokens are emitted.
  4. If a token is rejected, generation resumes from the correction point.

Diagram:

Draft model:   "the capital of France is Paris"
Target model:  checks all proposed tokens
Accepted:      "the capital of France is Paris"

If most draft tokens are accepted, speed improves.

Draft Model and Target Model

The target model is the model whose output quality you want.

The draft model is a cheaper predictor used to guess likely next tokens.

Classic speculative decoding uses a smaller model as the draft.

EAGLE-style methods use a draft head trained on the target model’s internal hidden states. That tends to match the target distribution better.

Verification Process

The target model verifies a sequence of proposed tokens.

If the draft proposes K tokens and the target accepts a fraction alpha, the expected number of useful tokens per target pass increases.

alpha is the acceptance rate.

Acceptance rate matters more than marketing speedup.

If alpha is high, speculative decoding helps. If alpha is low, the system wastes time drafting and rejecting.

EAGLE Architecture

EAGLE methods train a lightweight module to predict future tokens from target-model hidden states.

Why that helps:

  • the draft head is aligned with the target model
  • it is cheaper than running a separate large draft model
  • acceptance rates are often higher than classic small-model drafting

EAGLE-3 improves this by using richer target-model internal signals, not only the final hidden layer. The goal is to draft tokens that the target model is likely to accept.

Why EAGLE-3 Matters

EAGLE-3 matters because decode is a major bottleneck in production serving.

If EAGLE-3 raises accepted tokens per target forward, it can:

  • reduce inter-token latency
  • increase tokens per second
  • lower cost per generated token
  • improve user experience for long outputs

But it is not free.

It adds:

  • draft computation
  • verification logic
  • serving-engine complexity
  • quality validation requirement
  • tuning per workload

Practical Deployment

The production rollout should be staged:

  1. Measure baseline without speculative decoding.
  2. Enable EAGLE-3 or another speculative mode in a test environment.
  3. Measure acceptance rate on real traffic samples.
  4. Check mean and P99 inter-token latency.
  5. Check output quality.
  6. Roll out gradually.
  7. Disable if tail latency or quality regresses.

The gate:

Do not enable speculative decoding because it is available.
Enable it because acceptance rate and tail latency prove it helps.

Tradeoffs

Benefit Cost
faster decode when acceptance is high worse tail latency when acceptance is low
lower cost per token extra draft overhead
better user experience for long generations more complex serving configuration
strong fit for general chat may underperform on code, legal, medical, or domain text without tuning

Interview Insight

If someone asks “What is the most important metric for speculative decoding?”, answer:

Acceptance rate on the production traffic distribution.

Not benchmark speedup. Not draft model size. Not theoretical tokens per second. Acceptance rate under your workload.

Key Takeaways

  • Decoding is slow because generation is token-by-token.
  • Speculative decoding drafts multiple tokens and verifies them with the target model.
  • EAGLE-3 improves draft quality by using target-model hidden states.
  • Acceptance rate determines whether speculative decoding helps.
  • Tail latency can worsen if rejected drafts are common.

Common Mistakes

  • Turning on speculative decoding without measuring acceptance rate.
  • Testing only general chat while production traffic is code or domain-specific.
  • Looking at average inter-token latency but ignoring P99.
  • Combining incompatible serving flags without reading engine release notes.

Production Insights

  • Log accepted and rejected draft tokens.
  • Gate rollout on production-like traffic, not synthetic prompts.
  • Keep a safe fallback path without speculative decoding.
  • Consider domain-specific draft tuning for specialized workloads.

SGLang RadixAttention

Simple Intuition

SGLang saves work by remembering shared beginnings.

If 10,000 requests start with the same long system prompt and tool schema, the serving engine should not recompute that prefix 10,000 times.

RadixAttention stores reusable prefix state in a tree so future requests can reuse it.

What SGLang Is

SGLang is an LLM serving and programming system designed for efficient structured generation and prefix-heavy workloads.

It became important because many real applications are prefix-heavy:

  • RAG systems with repeated prompt templates
  • agents with repeated tool schemas
  • assistants with long system prompts
  • evaluation harnesses with fixed instructions
  • multi-user products with shared templates

The Prefix Sharing Problem

Suppose every request starts like this:

system prompt
tool schemas
format instructions
retrieval context header
user question

The first parts may be identical across requests. A human sees obvious reuse. A naive serving engine sees separate token sequences and recomputes them.

The waste can be enormous.

RadixAttention Internals

A radix tree is a compact prefix tree.

SGLang stores token prefixes in a radix tree. Each tree node owns KV cache blocks for the prefix along that path.

ASCII view:

root
  -> system prompt
      -> tool schemas
          -> document A
              -> question 1
              -> question 2
          -> document B
              -> question 3

If a new request shares system prompt -> tool schemas -> document A, it reuses the KV cache for that prefix and only computes the new suffix.

Tree-Based KV Cache Reuse

The key idea:

prefill cost = new suffix tokens, not full prompt tokens

If a 4,000-token prompt has a 3,500-token cached prefix, the engine only prefills 500 new tokens.

That saves:

  • compute
  • memory bandwidth
  • time to first token
  • cost

Cache-Aware Scheduling

Prefix caching only works well if the scheduler preserves locality.

First-come, first-served scheduling can destroy cache benefits by bouncing between unrelated prefixes.

SGLang’s scheduler prefers requests that share hot prefixes. This keeps useful branches resident in GPU memory.

The scheduler becomes cache-aware:

Which request should run next?
Not only the oldest one.
The one that best reuses resident cache while respecting fairness.

Why Prefix Caching Matters

Modern LLM applications repeat a lot of prompt structure.

Agents are the clearest example. Tool schemas can be thousands of tokens. If every agent step repeats the same tool descriptions, prefix caching can save a large fraction of prefill.

RAG systems also repeat:

  • system instructions
  • citation rules
  • answer format
  • document headers
  • tenant-specific policies

Prompt Ordering Discipline

Prefix caching depends on exact token order.

These two prompts may look similar to a human:

system -> tools -> context -> question
system -> context -> tools -> question

To a radix tree, they are different prefixes.

The production rule:

Put stable shared content first.
Put dynamic content later.
Put the most unique content last.

Good ordering:

system
tools
format rules
tenant policy
retrieved context
user question

Bad ordering:

user question
system
retrieved context
tools
format rules

Real-World Acceleration

SGLang shines when prefix hit rate is high.

Good workloads:

  • high-volume RAG with shared templates
  • agent loops with stable tool schemas
  • evaluation workloads
  • multi-tenant SaaS with repeated policies
  • voice or multimodal systems with repeated prompts

Weak workloads:

  • unique one-off prompts
  • highly dynamic prompt ordering
  • short prompts where prefill cost is small
  • workloads dominated by long decode rather than prefill

vLLM and SGLang

vLLM also supports prefix caching. The difference is emphasis.

vLLM is a general-purpose high-throughput serving engine. SGLang is more aggressively designed around structured programs, prefix reuse, and cache-aware scheduling.

The choice:

Workload Likely default
general chat vLLM
prefix-heavy RAG SGLang
tool-heavy agents SGLang or vLLM with careful prefix caching
broad self-hosted serving vLLM
structured generation pipelines SGLang

Key Takeaways

  • SGLang optimizes prefix-heavy workloads.
  • RadixAttention stores shared prefixes in a radix tree.
  • Cache-aware scheduling keeps hot prefixes resident.
  • Prompt ordering determines cache hit rate.
  • Prefix caching improves prefill cost, time to first token, and throughput.

Common Mistakes

  • Randomizing prompt component order across services.
  • Putting user-specific dynamic content before shared system content.
  • Measuring cache hit rate only in local tests.
  • Expecting prefix caching to help unique prompts.
  • Ignoring fairness when cache-aware scheduling prioritizes hot branches.

Production Insights

  • Treat prompt templates as cache keys.
  • Version prompt templates carefully.
  • Log prefix-cache hit rate by route and tenant.
  • Use SGLang when prompt reuse is structural, not accidental.
  • Reorder prompts before buying more GPUs.

TensorRT-LLM and Blackwell

Simple Intuition

TensorRT-LLM is what you use when you want NVIDIA hardware to run as close to its limits as possible.

If vLLM is a powerful general-purpose serving engine, TensorRT-LLM is a highly tuned race car built for NVIDIA tracks.

The tradeoff is clear:

more performance, less portability

TensorRT-LLM Architecture

TensorRT-LLM is NVIDIA’s optimized inference stack for large language models.

It includes:

  • graph compilation
  • optimized CUDA kernels
  • attention optimizations
  • quantization support
  • multi-GPU communication
  • tensor parallelism
  • pipeline parallelism
  • plugins for common LLM operations
  • integration with Triton Inference Server and NVIDIA Dynamo

It is built around NVIDIA GPUs and CUDA.

Kernel Fusion

A kernel is a GPU program. Kernel fusion combines multiple operations into one kernel.

Why it matters:

Every separate kernel launch has overhead. Every intermediate tensor may be written to and read from memory. Fusion reduces overhead and memory traffic.

Example:

without fusion:
  matrix multiply -> write output
  add bias -> write output
  apply activation -> write output

with fusion:
  matrix multiply + bias + activation -> write once

For LLM inference, reducing memory traffic is often as important as increasing compute.

Quantization

Quantization represents numbers with fewer bits.

Common formats:

Format Meaning Use
BF16 16-bit floating point quality-focused serving
FP8 8-bit floating point fast inference with good range
INT8 8-bit integer older quantized inference
4-bit very compressed weights cost-focused serving
NVFP4 NVIDIA 4-bit microscaling format Blackwell-optimized inference

Quantization reduces:

  • weight memory
  • memory bandwidth
  • cost per token

But it can hurt:

  • reasoning quality
  • long-context stability
  • code generation
  • math accuracy
  • rare-token behavior

Always validate quantized models on your eval suite.

FP8 and KV Cache

FP8 is especially important for key-value cache and attention because it preserves more dynamic range than 4-bit formats.

The key-value cache stores attention information across many tokens. If you quantize it too aggressively, attention quality can collapse.

Production stacks often use:

weights: lower precision
activations: lower precision
KV cache: FP8
accumulators: higher precision

This balances cost and quality.

Blackwell GPU Architecture

NVIDIA Blackwell GPUs, including B200 and GB200 systems, are designed for large-scale AI inference and training.

Important ideas:

  • faster tensor cores
  • higher memory bandwidth
  • support for low-precision formats
  • stronger multi-GPU interconnect
  • better mixture-of-experts communication
  • hardware support for new precision formats

Tensor cores are specialized units for matrix operations. LLMs are mostly matrix operations. Better tensor cores mean faster prefill and faster feed-forward layers.

Memory bandwidth matters because decode often reads large weights repeatedly.

Multi-GPU Inference

Large models may not fit on one GPU or may need more throughput than one GPU provides.

TensorRT-LLM supports multi-GPU inference through:

  • tensor parallelism
  • pipeline parallelism
  • expert parallelism for mixture-of-experts models
  • optimized communication kernels

On systems such as GB200 NVL72, many GPUs are connected with high-bandwidth interconnect. That enables large models to run as a tightly coupled system.

Performance Tuning

TensorRT-LLM performance tuning includes:

  • choosing precision
  • selecting parallelism strategy
  • tuning batch sizes
  • enabling optimized attention kernels
  • managing KV cache precision
  • using speculative or multi-token prediction
  • tuning communication collectives
  • matching model architecture to hardware

This is not a casual stack. It rewards teams with strong infrastructure skill.

Enterprise Deployment

Enterprise TensorRT-LLM deployments usually appear when:

  • token volume is extremely high
  • NVIDIA hardware is standardized
  • cost per token matters deeply
  • the team can operate specialized infrastructure
  • model set is stable enough to compile and optimize

Common pattern:

experimentation tier: vLLM on H100/H200
high-volume tier: TensorRT-LLM on Blackwell
managed tier: provider APIs for frontier models

Use TensorRT-LLM where performance pays for complexity.

Tradeoffs

TensorRT-LLM advantage Cost
high throughput NVIDIA lock-in
strong Blackwell support more specialized build pipeline
optimized kernels less flexibility
lower cost at high scale more infra expertise required
enterprise NVIDIA support slower iteration than pure Python stacks

Key Takeaways

  • TensorRT-LLM is NVIDIA’s optimized LLM inference stack.
  • Blackwell improves low-precision inference and multi-GPU serving.
  • FP8 remains important for KV cache and attention.
  • NVFP4 can reduce weight and activation cost but needs quality validation.
  • TensorRT-LLM trades portability for performance.

Common Mistakes

  • Assuming 4-bit quantization is safe for every workload.
  • Migrating to TensorRT-LLM before workload volume justifies it.
  • Ignoring model compilation and deployment complexity.
  • Comparing TensorRT-LLM and vLLM without matching hardware generation.
  • Failing to run domain evals after quantization.

Production Insights

  • Keep a flexible serving tier for fast model iteration.
  • Use TensorRT-LLM for stable, high-volume workloads.
  • Treat quantization as a model change, not a deployment flag.
  • Benchmark quality, latency, throughput, and cost together.
  • NVIDIA lock-in is acceptable only when the economics justify it.

Disaggregated Prefill/Decode

Simple Intuition

Prefill and decode are different jobs.

Prefill is like reading a whole book quickly to understand the context. Decode is like writing one word at a time while constantly checking your notes.

Running both on the same kind of GPU pool is convenient, but not always efficient.

Disaggregated serving separates them:

prefill pool -> KV cache transfer -> decode pool

Prefill vs Decode

Prefill:

  • processes all input tokens
  • benefits from large matrix multiplications
  • is compute-bound
  • likes GPUs with strong compute throughput

Decode:

  • generates one token at a time
  • repeatedly reads weights and KV cache
  • is often memory-bandwidth-bound
  • likes high memory bandwidth and high concurrency

Colocating both phases means one GPU pool handles two different bottlenecks.

Architecture

Disaggregated architecture:

Client
  -> Router
      -> Prefill workers
          -> KV cache transfer
      -> Decode workers
          -> token stream

ASCII:

             prompt
               |
               v
        +--------------+
        | Prefill Pool |
        +--------------+
               |
        KV cache transfer
               |
               v
        +-------------+
        | Decode Pool |
        +-------------+
               |
            tokens

The prefill worker computes the prompt’s KV cache. The decode worker uses that cache to generate output tokens.

Compute-Bound vs Memory-Bound

Compute-bound means the limiting resource is arithmetic throughput.

Memory-bound means the limiting resource is moving data.

Prefill is compute-heavy because it processes many prompt tokens in parallel.

Decode is memory-heavy because each step uses large model weights to produce one token.

Disaggregation lets you size pools differently:

more prefill GPUs for long-prompt workloads
more decode GPUs for long-output workloads

Network Communication

The cost is KV cache transfer.

The prefill pool must send KV cache to the decode pool. That requires fast networking:

  • NVLink inside a node
  • InfiniBand across nodes
  • RDMA for low-latency transfer
  • TCP fallback when nothing better exists

RDMA means Remote Direct Memory Access. It lets machines transfer data directly between memory regions with less CPU overhead.

If the KV transfer is slow, disaggregation can lose.

When It Helps

Disaggregation helps when:

  • prompts are long
  • outputs are moderate or long
  • traffic mix is skewed
  • prefill and decode need different scaling
  • network is fast
  • request volume is large enough to justify complexity

It often helps RAG, long-context assistants, and reasoning models with heavy prompts.

When It Hurts

It can hurt when:

  • prompts are short
  • outputs are short
  • cluster is small
  • network is slow
  • traffic is low
  • operational team is not ready for two serving pools

The rule:

If KV transfer costs more than saved prefill/decode efficiency, do not disaggregate.

NVIDIA Dynamo and llm-d

NVIDIA Dynamo is an orchestration layer for disaggregated serving across engines such as vLLM, SGLang, and TensorRT-LLM.

It manages:

  • prefill/decode pool sizing
  • routing
  • KV transfer
  • workload planning
  • service-level objectives

llm-d is a Kubernetes-native approach associated with Red Hat and AWS work. It models prefill, decode, and routing as separate Kubernetes services with separate autoscaling signals.

The practical difference:

Stack Best for
NVIDIA Dynamo NVIDIA-optimized deployments and high-performance orchestration
llm-d Kubernetes-native shops that want explicit services and autoscaling

Scheduling Improvements

Disaggregation improves scheduling because prefill and decode queues can be managed separately.

Prefill scaling signal:

waiting prompt tokens
queue depth
time to first token

Decode scaling signal:

active sequences
KV cache pressure
inter-token latency

One autoscaler cannot manage both cleanly because the phases behave differently.

Key Takeaways

  • Prefill and decode have different bottlenecks.
  • Disaggregated serving places them on separate infrastructure pools.
  • KV cache transfer is the main cost.
  • It helps long-prompt and large-scale workloads.
  • It adds operational complexity and networking requirements.

Common Mistakes

  • Disaggregating short-prompt workloads.
  • Ignoring network transfer cost.
  • Using one autoscaling signal for both prefill and decode.
  • Deploying disaggregation before mastering colocated serving.
  • Forgetting failure handling during KV transfer.

Production Insights

  • Start with colocated serving. Move to disaggregation when measurements justify it.
  • Track prefill queue depth and decode KV pressure separately.
  • Place prefill and decode pools close in network topology.
  • Use disaggregation for high-volume long-context workloads, not as a default.

vLLM Production Stack and LMCache

Simple Intuition

The vLLM engine is the worker. A production stack is the whole factory.

You need routers, metrics, deployment controls, cache layers, failure handling, and multi-node coordination. LMCache adds an external memory layer for KV cache so GPUs do not have to recompute expensive prefixes or lose work when memory pressure rises.

Full Production Serving Architecture

A production LLM serving stack often looks like this:

Client
  -> API Gateway
  -> Auth / rate limits
  -> AI Router
  -> Scheduler
  -> vLLM engines
  -> KV cache layer
  -> Observability
  -> Billing / FinOps

In Kubernetes:

Ingress
  -> gateway service
  -> router deployment
  -> vLLM engine deployments
  -> LMCache service
  -> Prometheus / Grafana / tracing

API Gateway

The API gateway handles:

  • authentication
  • request validation
  • rate limiting
  • tenant identification
  • request logging
  • routing headers
  • abuse protection

It is the front door. Do not put all policy inside the model server.

Router

The router decides where a request goes.

Routing can consider:

  • model name
  • region
  • tenant
  • engine health
  • queue depth
  • cache locality
  • LoRA adapter loaded
  • context length
  • cost policy

For cache-heavy workloads, routing is not simple load balancing. The best engine may be the one with the right KV cache blocks already warm.

Scheduler

Inside each vLLM engine, the scheduler handles admission, batching, prefill, decode, and KV cache pressure.

Across engines, the router or control plane handles global scheduling:

which engine gets this request?
which engine has capacity?
which engine has cache?
which engine is safe for this tenant?

KV Cache Storage

GPU high-bandwidth memory is precious. KV cache can consume it quickly.

LMCache moves some KV cache outside GPU memory:

GPU HBM -> CPU DRAM -> disk or distributed storage

This is a cache hierarchy.

Tier Fast Large Expensive
GPU HBM yes small very
CPU DRAM medium larger moderate
disk/object store slow huge cheaper

LMCache is useful when:

  • KV cache exceeds GPU memory
  • requests are preempted
  • prefixes repeat across engines
  • long contexts recur
  • multiple engines share templates

External KV Cache

An external KV cache lets one engine reuse work computed by another engine.

Without external cache:

engine A prefills prefix
engine B later prefills same prefix again

With external cache:

engine A stores prefix KV
engine B loads prefix KV

The tradeoff is transfer latency and cache consistency.

Multi-Node Inference

Multi-node serving adds:

  • network transfer
  • distributed scheduling
  • shared state
  • partial failure
  • placement constraints

KV cache reuse across nodes depends on bandwidth and latency. Moving hundreds of megabytes of cache over a slow network may be worse than recomputing.

Persistent Cache Reuse

Persistent cache reuse matters for workloads such as:

  • repeated RAG prefixes
  • repeated system prompts
  • common documents
  • evaluation suites
  • agent tool schemas
  • tenant policies

The serving system can avoid prefill for cache hits. That improves time to first token and lowers cost.

Failure Handling

What if LMCache fails?

The safe fallback:

cache unavailable -> recompute prefill -> continue serving

The system should degrade, not fail completely.

Production cache layers need:

  • replication
  • health checks
  • timeouts
  • fallback to recompute
  • cache invalidation rules
  • versioning for prompt templates
  • tenant isolation

Scaling Across GPU Clusters

At large scale, one cluster may not be enough.

You may need:

  • regional clusters
  • separate GPU generations
  • separate models
  • dedicated enterprise capacity
  • batch clusters
  • experimental clusters

The router becomes a policy engine:

send low-latency chat to nearest warm cluster
send batch jobs to cheap off-peak cluster
send regulated traffic to approved region
send long-context workloads to high-memory pool

Key Takeaways

  • A production serving stack is more than a model server.
  • Routers, gateways, schedulers, cache layers, and observability are part of serving.
  • LMCache externalizes KV cache to reduce recomputation and handle memory pressure.
  • External cache helps most when prefixes repeat or KV pressure exceeds GPU memory.
  • Cache failures should degrade to recomputation, not outage.

Common Mistakes

  • Running vLLM directly on the public internet.
  • Using round-robin routing for cache-heavy workloads.
  • Treating LMCache as free.
  • Forgetting tenant isolation in shared cache.
  • Making cache a hard dependency with no fallback.

Production Insights

  • Put API policy at the gateway, not in the model server.
  • Route by model, tenant, cache locality, queue depth, and region.
  • Track cache hit rate, cache load latency, recompute rate, and preemption events.
  • Use external KV cache only after measuring HBM pressure and prefix reuse.
  • Design cache failure as a performance degradation, not a correctness failure.

Pulling the Stack Together

A modern serving architecture usually has several tiers.

                 +------------------+
Client traffic ->|   API Gateway    |
                 +------------------+
                          |
                          v
                 +------------------+
                 |   AI Router      |
                 +------------------+
                    |       |       |
                    v       v       v
              +--------+ +--------+ +--------+
              | vLLM   | | SGLang | | TRT-LLM|
              | pool   | | pool   | | pool   |
              +--------+ +--------+ +--------+
                    |       |       |
                    +---+---+-------+
                        v
                 +------------------+
                 | KV Cache / Logs  |
                 +------------------+
                        |
                        v
                 +------------------+
                 | Observability    |
                 +------------------+

The router chooses the right path:

  • managed API for frontier model access
  • vLLM for general self-hosted serving
  • SGLang for prefix-heavy workloads
  • TensorRT-LLM for high-volume NVIDIA-optimized workloads
  • disaggregated serving for long-context scale
  • LMCache when KV cache reuse or pressure justifies it

The engineering skill is knowing when not to use the fancy path.

For a low-volume prototype, call a managed API.

For a growing product, add a gateway and observability.

For stable high-volume workloads, evaluate self-hosted vLLM or SGLang.

For extremely high-volume NVIDIA-heavy deployments, evaluate TensorRT-LLM on Blackwell.

For long-context and RAG-heavy traffic, evaluate disaggregated serving and external KV cache.

Chapter Summary

Serving LLMs at scale is the foundation of AI in production.

The model is only one part of the system. The serving layer decides latency, throughput, cost, reliability, and operational control.

Managed platforms give speed and enterprise abstraction. Inference economics tells you when the bill will hurt. Kubernetes lets you run GPU infrastructure, but only if you use GPU-aware scheduling and correct autoscaling signals. vLLM gives a strong general-purpose serving engine. EAGLE-3 attacks decode latency. SGLang attacks repeated prefix cost. TensorRT-LLM and Blackwell attack cost per token at high scale. Disaggregated prefill/decode attacks phase mismatch. LMCache attacks KV cache pressure and recomputation.

The production rule:

Start simple. Measure honestly. Add complexity only when the bottleneck proves it is needed.

Final Key Takeaways

  • LLM serving is a distributed systems problem under GPU economics.
  • Prefill and decode are different phases with different bottlenecks.
  • Managed platforms are the fastest path to production but reduce control.
  • Cost is dominated by utilization, token volume, model size, and memory bandwidth.
  • Kubernetes needs GPU-aware scheduling, not generic CPU autoscaling habits.
  • vLLM is the default open-source serving engine because of PagedAttention and continuous batching.
  • EAGLE-3 helps only when acceptance rate is strong on real traffic.
  • SGLang wins when prefix reuse is structural.
  • TensorRT-LLM on Blackwell can be economically decisive at high scale, but it locks you into NVIDIA.
  • Disaggregated serving and LMCache are advanced tools for workloads where measurements justify them.

Final Common Mistakes

  • Building self-hosted infrastructure before validating product demand.
  • Staying on managed APIs after the workload is large and stable enough to optimize.
  • Treating average latency as the user experience.
  • Ignoring KV cache as a first-class resource.
  • Scaling on GPU duty cycle instead of queue and latency signals.
  • Enabling every serving optimization at once without isolating effects.
  • Optimizing cost by damaging reliability.

Final Production Insights

  • The best LLM serving teams measure everything: TTFT, ITL, goodput, queue depth, KV utilization, cost per success, cache hit rate, and tail latency.
  • Architecture should follow workload shape. Chat, RAG, agents, code, batch, and voice should not blindly share one serving policy.
  • Every advanced optimization has a workload where it helps and a workload where it hurts.
  • Serving decisions should be revisited whenever models, traffic, context lengths, or product expectations change.
  • In production AI, the serving stack is part of the product.

Chapter 2 - Inference Economics and Optimization

The first time you run an LLM locally, inference feels simple. You load weights, send a prompt, and tokens appear. The first time you run an LLM for real users, inference stops feeling simple very quickly.

One user wants the first token immediately. Another sends a 60,000-token legal contract. A third opens ten browser tabs that all call the same assistant. Your finance team asks why the GPU bill doubled. Your product team asks why latency is good in the morning and terrible after lunch. Your security team asks whether prompts are leaving the region. Your support team says customers are seeing timeouts, but the dashboard says average latency is fine.

This is inference economics.

The problem is not just “make the model faster.” The real problem is:

  • deliver useful tokens under a latency target
  • keep GPUs busy without making users wait forever
  • reduce memory pressure without breaking quality
  • reuse computation when possible
  • route simple work away from expensive models
  • observe every layer well enough to debug failures
  • control cost per useful answer, not just cost per raw token

Training is a capital project. Inference is an operating business. You pay for it every day. A training run ends. Serving does not.

This chapter covers the part of production AI engineering where the model becomes an economic machine. You will learn how to measure inference properly, optimize it with quantization and caching, avoid cold-start disasters, route traffic across regions and models, use batch processing when latency does not matter, and make the system observable enough to operate.

The theme is simple: in production, optimization is not a trick. It is an architecture.

The Optimization Mindset

Before we go into individual techniques, you need the mental model.

An LLM serving system has four scarce resources:

  1. GPU compute
  2. GPU memory
  3. memory bandwidth
  4. user patience

Most beginners optimize only the first one. They ask, “How many tokens per second can this GPU generate?” That number matters, but it is not enough. A system can produce many tokens per second while still being terrible for users. It can have high aggregate throughput and awful tail latency. It can have excellent average latency and still fail every enterprise contract because P99 latency crosses the service-level objective.

Inference optimization is the discipline of spending scarce resources on the requests that matter.

Here is the serving stack you should keep in your head:

client
  |
  v
API gateway
  |
  v
router / policy layer
  |
  v
queue / scheduler
  |
  v
model worker
  |
  +--> tokenizer
  +--> prefill
  +--> decode loop
  +--> KV cache
  +--> streaming response
  |
  v
observability + billing + audit logs

Every optimization in this chapter touches one or more parts of this path.

Goodput metrics improve the measurement layer. Quantization changes model memory and compute. Cold-start mitigation changes worker readiness. Multi-region KV locality changes routing. Edge inference changes the deployment location. Observability makes the whole path debuggable. Prompt and semantic caching remove work. Batch APIs change the user contract. Model routing avoids using expensive models when cheaper ones are enough.

There is no universal best setting. The correct design depends on your workload:

Workload Main pressure Common default
Chat assistant Time to first token and steady streaming low TTFT, continuous batching, prefix cache
Coding agent long context and tool latency KV locality, prompt cache, good tracing
Document extraction throughput and cost batch API, structured outputs, cheap model route
Voice assistant inter-token latency small model, edge option, aggressive warm pools
Enterprise RAG privacy and predictable latency regional routing, observability, semantic cache
Offline evaluation cost per token batch API, quantization, low-priority queues

The rest of the chapter builds the toolkit.

1. Inference Metrics and Goodput

Intuition

If you measure the wrong thing, you optimize the wrong system.

LLM inference looks like a restaurant kitchen. A customer does not care how many meals the kitchen cooked in total today. They care whether their meal arrived hot, correct, and on time. The restaurant owner cares about total meals per hour, but if the kitchen increases throughput by making half the customers wait forty minutes, the business is worse.

Raw throughput is kitchen output. Latency is customer waiting time. Goodput is the number of successful customer experiences delivered under the promised constraints.

That distinction matters because LLM serving is full of tradeoffs. You can increase throughput by batching more requests together, but that may increase queue latency. You can reduce GPU idle time by waiting longer before dispatching a batch, but that may destroy time to first token. You can run a larger model for better quality, but your cost per useful answer may become unacceptable.

Good inference metrics force the serving stack to optimize for what users and the business actually experience.

The Core Problem

An LLM request has multiple phases. Treating it as one latency number hides the failure mode.

request arrives
  |
  +--> queue wait
  |
  +--> tokenization
  |
  +--> prefill: process prompt tokens
  |
  +--> first token emitted
  |
  +--> decode: generate one token at a time
  |
  +--> final token emitted

The important latency terms are:

Metric Simple definition Why it matters
Time to First Token (TTFT) time from request arrival to first generated token determines perceived responsiveness
Inter-token latency (ITL) delay between streamed output tokens determines whether streaming feels smooth
Time per Output Token (TPOT) average decode time per generated token measures decode speed
End-to-end latency (E2E) full request time from arrival to completion matters for non-streaming and workflows
Queue latency time spent waiting before execution reveals overload and batching side effects
Tail latency high-percentile latency such as P95/P99 shows worst-user experience
Throughput total tokens produced per second measures system capacity
Cost per token serving cost divided by tokens connects engineering to finance
Success rate fraction of requests that complete correctly catches timeouts, 5xxs, and policy failures
Goodput fraction of requests satisfying all service targets best measure of useful capacity

For an interactive chat system, TTFT might matter more than total latency. For a batch summarization job, throughput and cost per token matter more. For a voice assistant, inter-token latency is brutal: a technically correct answer that arrives in uneven chunks feels broken.

What Happens Internally

Time to first token is usually:

TTFT = queue_time + network_request_time + prefill_time

Prefill is the phase where the model processes the entire input prompt and builds the key-value cache. Long prompts make prefill expensive. This is why a 50-token chat message and a 50,000-token document question behave completely differently.

After prefill, the model enters decode. Decode is usually:

E2E = TTFT + TPOT * output_tokens + network_response_time

Decode is token-by-token. The model generates one output token, appends it to the context, uses the KV cache, and generates the next token. This serial dependency is why LLM inference is difficult. You can parallelize computation inside one token, but token 200 depends on token 199.

Throughput is:

throughput = total_output_tokens / elapsed_time

That number is useful, but dangerous alone. A server can achieve excellent throughput by allowing a large queue to build up. Users would experience poor TTFT even though the GPU looks efficient.

Goodput fixes this by counting only requests that satisfy the contract.
