  • model aliases auto-upgrade silently
  • eval results are not attached to versions
  • rollback target is unknown
  • deployment config lives only in Kubernetes YAML

This is how teams lose reproducibility.

Key Takeaways

  • A production model version is a bundle of artifacts and configuration.
  • Registry entries should use immutable digests.
  • Deployment manifests make runtime behavior explicit.
  • Prompt versions must be tracked like code.

Common Mistakes

  • Using model aliases as deployment references.
  • Rolling back weights but not prompts or routing policy.
  • Treating tokenizer files as secondary.
  • Editing prompts directly in production dashboards.
  • Not storing eval results with the artifact.

Production Insights

Rollback works only if the previous known-good version still exists as a complete deployable bundle. If rollback requires guessing which tokenizer, prompt, or quantization config was used, rollback is not real.

Industry Best Practices

  • Pin digests, not names.
  • Store eval results with model versions.
  • Version prompts and tool schemas.
  • Require review for production prompt changes.
  • Generate deployment manifests from source-controlled config.
  • Keep previous versions warm or quickly loadable for rollback.

5. Shadow Traffic

Intuition

Shadow traffic means sending a copy of real production requests to a candidate model without showing its answers to users.

The production model still serves the user. The candidate model runs quietly in the background. You compare behavior, latency, output length, errors, refusals, and cost.

Shadow mode is a smoke test with real traffic distribution and zero user impact.

The Core Problem

Offline evals are limited. They use fixed datasets. Production traffic is messy.

Real users send:

  • unexpected languages
  • malformed prompts
  • long documents
  • strange formatting
  • adversarial inputs
  • empty messages
  • tool-heavy workflows
  • tenant-specific jargon

Shadow traffic shows whether the candidate survives the real distribution.

Architecture

live request
  |
  v
gateway
  |
  +--> production model -> response to user
  |
  +--> shadow candidate -> log only

The shadow path must not affect user latency. If the candidate is slow, the user should not wait.

Shadow logging should include:

  • prompt hash or redacted prompt
  • production model output
  • candidate output
  • token counts
  • latency
  • refusal status
  • error status
  • tool-call validity
  • cost estimate
  • evaluation score if available

What Shadow Catches

Shadow mode catches:

  • hard errors
  • provider incompatibility
  • latency regression
  • cost increase
  • output length explosion
  • refusal behavior shift
  • invalid tool calls
  • cache behavior problems

Shadow mode does not fully catch user preference. Users never see the shadow answer, so you cannot measure conversion, retention, trust, or satisfaction directly.

Shadow Comparison

Comparison can use:

  • exact schema validation
  • output length distribution
  • LLM-as-judge on sampled outputs
  • deterministic task validators
  • refusal classifiers
  • cost deltas
  • latency percentiles

Do not overtrust LLM judges. They are useful for triage, not absolute truth.

Production Bottlenecks

Shadow traffic doubles some work. If you shadow 100% of production to an expensive candidate, you may double cost. You may also overload providers or self-hosted clusters.

Use sampling:

shadow 1% first
shadow 10% after basic stability
shadow high-risk tenants separately
shadow long-context traffic with budget caps

Key Takeaways

  • Shadow mode tests candidate models on real traffic without user impact.
  • It catches distribution, latency, cost, and formatting problems.
  • It is not a replacement for A/B testing.
  • Shadow traffic must be sampled and budgeted.

Common Mistakes

  • Letting shadow latency affect live user latency.
  • Shadowing all traffic without cost controls.
  • Comparing only average output quality.
  • Ignoring output length and refusal shifts.
  • Treating shadow success as proof users prefer the model.

Production Insights

Shadow mode is especially valuable for cost surprises. A candidate model may pass quality evals but produce longer answers, call more tools, or miss cache more often. Users may never see those outputs, but finance will.

Industry Best Practices

  • Start with low-percentage shadow traffic.
  • Separate shadow metrics from production metrics.
  • Gate canary on shadow cost, latency, errors, refusals, and schema validity.
  • Redact sensitive data before storing shadow outputs.
  • Use shadow findings to update offline evals.

6. Canary and Progressive Rollouts

Intuition

A canary rollout exposes a small slice of real users to the new version before everyone gets it.

The idea comes from using a canary as an early warning system. In software, the canary is a small percentage of traffic. If metrics degrade, stop and roll back before the blast radius grows.

For LLM systems, canary is not optional. Model changes can fail in subtle ways.

The Core Problem

An LLM release can pass offline evals and still fail production because:

  • live prompts differ from eval prompts
  • output length increases cost
  • latency changes under batch pressure
  • refusal behavior shifts
  • user preference changes
  • tool calls fail on real schemas
  • model routing sends different traffic than expected

Canary rollout limits exposure while collecting production evidence.

Rollout Progression

A common progression:

1% -> 10% -> 25% -> 50% -> 75% -> 100%

Each step must pass gates before moving forward.

Canary Gates

LLM-specific canary gates should include:

Gate Example breach
latency P99 TTFT > 1.5x baseline
cost cost/request > 20% above baseline
error rate 5xx or timeouts > 2x baseline
refusal rate refusal rate > 2x baseline
output length P99 output tokens shifts materially
user feedback thumbs-down > 1.5x baseline
tool validity invalid tool calls above threshold
cache hit rate prefix cache hit drops sharply

Do not rely on one metric. A model can be faster and cheaper but worse. It can be better and too expensive. It can be high quality but unsafe.

Non-Determinism and Noise

LLM outputs vary even for identical inputs.

Reasons:

  • sampling
  • batch-size differences
  • floating-point non-associativity
  • provider implementation changes
  • nondeterministic kernels

The Phase 17 lesson notes that run-to-run variation can be significant. The release lesson is practical: set gates above the noise floor. If your eval metric naturally moves by 7%, do not create a 2% rollback threshold.

Rollback Must Be Fast

Rollback should be a policy flip, not a redeploy.

Bad rollback:

edit code -> rebuild image -> deploy -> wait -> verify

Good rollback:

set canary_percent=0
set model_ref=previous_digest
reload gateway policy

If rollback takes hours, the release system is not production-grade.

Tooling

Common tooling:

  • feature flags: LaunchDarkly, Unleash, Flagsmith
  • Kubernetes progressive delivery: Argo Rollouts, Flagger
  • service mesh routing: Istio, Linkerd
  • model serving canary: KServe, Seldon
  • gateway-level traffic splitting

For LLM systems, gateway-level control is often the cleanest because the gateway already understands tenant, model, provider, and route policy.

Key Takeaways

  • Canary exposes a small percentage of users before full rollout.
  • LLM canaries need quality, cost, latency, refusal, and output-length gates.
  • Non-determinism means gates must account for natural variance.
  • Rollback should take seconds.

Common Mistakes

  • Jumping from shadow directly to 100%.
  • Gating only on HTTP errors.
  • Ignoring cost per request.
  • Making rollback depend on redeployment.
  • Progressing canary before enough samples accumulate.

Production Insights

The canary percentage is less important than the gate quality. A 1% canary with no meaningful gates is theater. A 10% canary with strong cost, latency, quality, and refusal monitoring is useful.

Industry Best Practices

  • Use 1%, 10%, 25%, 50%, 75%, 100% progression for risky changes.
  • Pause long enough to collect samples at each step.
  • Automate rollback for hard SLO breaches.
  • Keep model and policy rollback separate from code deploys.
  • Document every rollout decision.

7. Blue-Green Deployments

Intuition

Blue-green deployment keeps two production environments:

  • blue: current live environment
  • green: new candidate environment

You prepare green while blue serves users. When green is ready, traffic shifts from blue to green. If green fails, traffic shifts back.

This is useful when the change is too large for in-place rollout or when you need a fully warmed environment before switching.

Architecture

             +--> blue environment: current
gateway -----|
             +--> green environment: candidate

For LLMs, “environment” may include:

  • model workers
  • KV cache policy
  • gateway route config
  • prompt version
  • retrieval index
  • inference engine version
  • observability pipeline

When to Use Blue-Green

Use blue-green for:

  • inference engine upgrades
  • quantization format changes
  • model architecture changes
  • major Kubernetes/runtime changes
  • retrieval index migration
  • safety policy rewrite
  • provider migration

Canary is better for gradual model exposure. Blue-green is better for environment-level replacement.

LLM-Specific Challenges

Blue-green costs more because both environments may need GPUs.

If the green environment runs a 70B model, keeping it fully warm can be expensive. But for high-risk changes, the cost is justified.

KV cache does not automatically move from blue to green. After switch, TTFT may spike because caches are cold. You can mitigate this with:

  • prewarming common prompts
  • gradual traffic shift
  • shared external KV cache where supported
  • accepting temporary latency increase

Key Takeaways

  • Blue-green keeps old and new environments side by side.
  • It is useful for large infrastructure changes.
  • It costs more because duplicate capacity may be needed.
  • KV cache coldness is a real LLM-specific issue.

Common Mistakes

  • Treating blue-green as free.
  • Switching all traffic without warming green.
  • Forgetting retrieval index and prompt versions.
  • Assuming cache state transfers automatically.
  • Keeping green unobserved until cutover.

Production Insights

Blue-green is an insurance policy. You pay extra capacity for a short time so rollback is a routing decision instead of a rebuild. For critical enterprise deployments, that is often the right trade.

Industry Best Practices

  • Warm green before traffic shift.
  • Run smoke tests against green with real prompts.
  • Keep blue alive until green passes post-cutover checks.
  • Monitor cache hit rate after cutover.
  • Use blue-green for engine and infrastructure changes, not every prompt edit.

8. A/B Testing LLM Features

Intuition

Evals answer: can the model do the job?

A/B tests answer: do users care?

Both are required. A model can score better offline but make the product worse. It might be slower, more verbose, less trusted, too expensive, or worse for a key user segment.

An A/B test randomly assigns users to variants and measures product impact.

The Core Problem

LLM changes often affect subjective experience.

Examples:

  • a friendlier prompt increases engagement but lowers task efficiency
  • a cheaper model reduces cost but increases support tickets
  • a longer answer improves ratings for beginners but annoys experts
  • a more cautious safety policy reduces risk but increases false refusals

Offline evals cannot answer these product questions.

What to Test

Change Primary metric Guardrails
prompt wording task success, retention cost, refusal rate
model selection resolution rate latency, cost, safety
temperature/top-p user preference invalid outputs
retrieval strategy answer correctness hallucination, latency
tool policy completion rate tool errors, cost
response length satisfaction output tokens, time

One experiment should have one primary metric. You can track many guardrails, but you need a decision metric.

Evals vs A/B Tests

Method Offline or online Answers
Eval offline can the system perform the task?
Shadow production traffic, no exposure does candidate survive real distribution?
Canary online limited exposure is the release stable?
A/B test online randomized exposure do users prefer or behave better?

Canary is about safety. A/B is about product impact.

Statistical Issues

LLM experiments are noisy.

Reasons:

  • output variation
  • user heterogeneity
  • multiple metrics
  • repeated peeking
  • small effect sizes
  • provider drift

Useful tools:

  • CUPED: uses pre-experiment data to reduce variance
  • sequential testing: allows early stopping with controlled false positives
  • sample ratio mismatch checks: detect broken assignment
  • multiple-comparison corrections: avoid false wins when testing many variants

The Phase 17 source notes that LLM non-determinism may require a sample-size buffer. In practice, plan for more samples than a normal web A/B test if the model output itself adds variance.

Platform Choices

Examples from the source material:

Platform Fit
Statsig hosted all-in-one experimentation and feature flags
GrowthBook open-source, warehouse-native experimentation

The choice is less important than discipline:

  • stable random assignment
  • clear metrics
  • guardrails
  • enough sample size
  • no shipping on vibes

Production Bottlenecks

Common A/B failures:

  • experiment changes model and prompt simultaneously
  • no primary metric
  • too many variants
  • sample ratio mismatch
  • stopping early after a lucky day
  • ignoring cost and latency
  • measuring request-level instead of user-level outcomes

User-level randomization is usually better than request-level randomization for product metrics. A user who sees both variants can contaminate behavior.

Key Takeaways

  • Evals measure capability; A/B tests measure user impact.
  • Canary and A/B have different jobs.
  • LLM experiments need guardrails for cost, latency, safety, and quality.
  • Statistical discipline matters because output behavior is noisy.

Common Mistakes

  • Shipping because the new answer “feels better.”
  • Running A/B without enough sample size.
  • Testing many prompt variants without correction.
  • Ignoring sample ratio mismatch.
  • Optimizing engagement while cost explodes.

Production Insights

A/B tests often reveal that better model quality does not automatically mean better product metrics. Sometimes latency matters more. Sometimes shorter answers win. Sometimes users prefer consistency over brilliance.

Industry Best Practices

  • Run offline evals before online tests.
  • Canary for stability before A/B for product impact.
  • Define one primary metric and several guardrails.
  • Use CUPED or sequential testing where available.
  • Analyze by segment: new users, power users, enterprise tenants, language.

9. Load Testing LLM APIs

Intuition

Load testing asks: what happens when traffic gets heavy?

For normal APIs, you measure request latency and error rate. For LLM APIs, you also need token-level behavior, streaming latency, prompt length distribution, cache effects, GPU saturation, and queue dynamics.

Generic load testing can lie to you if it does not understand LLMs.

The Core Problem

LLM traffic is not uniform.

Requests vary by:

  • input tokens
  • output tokens
  • streaming duration
  • cache hit probability
  • tenant
  • model route
  • tool calls
  • prompt prefix

If your load test sends the same prompt 10,000 times, the server may hit prefix cache constantly. You think capacity is high. Production later fails because real prompts are diverse.

The Prompt-Uniformity Trap

Bad load test:

for i in 1..10000:
  send "Write a poem about Kubernetes"

This tests one prompt, one length, one prefix, one output shape.

Better:

sample prompt length from distribution
sample task type
sample tenant
sample output length target
sample cacheable vs non-cacheable prefix

Use real traffic samples when allowed. Otherwise build a synthetic distribution from realistic sources.

The Client Bottleneck Trap

Some load generators become the bottleneck. The Phase 17 lesson calls out the Python GIL trap in Locust when tokenization and request generation compete under heavy concurrency.

If the client cannot generate load correctly, the server metrics are meaningless.

Use tools that support streaming and token-level metrics, or distribute the load generator.

Load Patterns

Pattern Description Catches
steady-state constant RPS for 30-60 min baseline regression
ramp gradually increase traffic capacity breakpoint
spike sudden 3-10x traffic autoscaling and queue collapse
soak hours of traffic memory leaks, drift

Each pattern finds different failures. A five-minute happy-path test is not enough.

Metrics to Collect

Collect:

  • TTFT P50/P95/P99
  • TPOT P50/P95/P99
  • E2E latency
  • queue time
  • error rate
  • timeout rate
  • token throughput
  • GPU utilization
  • GPU memory
  • cache hit rate
  • cost per request
  • output length distribution
  • route distribution

The load test should fail if the release violates SLOs.

Tooling

The source lesson names:

  • LLMPerf
  • NVIDIA GenAI-Perf
  • LLM-Locust
  • guidellm
  • k6 with streaming-aware support
  • Vegeta for simpler HTTP saturation

Use LLM-specific tools for model-serving benchmarks. Use k6 or similar tools for CI/CD gates and gateway tests. Use several tools if needed, but keep metric definitions consistent.

CI Gate

A small CI load gate might run:

traffic: 30-50 realistic requests
concurrency: low to moderate
gate:
  TTFT P95 under threshold
  TPOT P95 under threshold
  5xx < 5%
  no schema failures

A pre-production gate should be larger:

steady-state: 60 minutes
ramp: 0 to target over 15 minutes
spike: 5x for 2 minutes
soak: 4-8 hours for major engine changes

Key Takeaways

  • LLM load testing must measure token-level streaming behavior.
  • Prompt distributions must be realistic.
  • Generic tools can lie if the client becomes the bottleneck.
  • CI should include performance gates before release.

Common Mistakes

  • Testing one repeated prompt.
  • Measuring only HTTP response completion.
  • Ignoring TTFT and TPOT.
  • Running load tests with cache behavior unlike production.
  • Forgetting spike and soak tests.

Production Insights

The fastest way to fool yourself is to benchmark a cache-friendly prompt loop. Real production has messy prompts, variable lengths, and uneven tenants. Test that.

Industry Best Practices

  • Build load profiles from real traffic distributions.
  • Separate gateway load tests from model-worker load tests.
  • Run spike tests before relying on autoscaling.
  • Include soak tests for engine/runtime changes.
  • Store load-test results with the release artifact.

10. CI/CD for AI Services

Intuition

Continuous integration and continuous delivery for AI should answer:

Is this change correct enough, safe enough, fast enough, cheap enough, and reversible enough to ship?

Traditional CI checks code. AI CI/CD checks code plus prompts, models, tools, policies, evals, latency, cost, and rollout config.

The Core Problem

AI changes are often not code changes.

A production behavior change can come from:

  • prompt update
  • model alias update
  • provider-side model change
  • retrieval index update
  • tool schema change
  • quantization change
  • gateway routing policy
  • safety policy
  • inference engine version

If CI/CD watches only application code, it misses the most important changes.

Pipeline Architecture

pull request
  |
  +--> lint and unit tests
  +--> prompt schema validation
  +--> tool schema validation
  +--> offline evals
  +--> safety tests
  +--> cost simulation
  +--> load smoke test
  +--> manifest validation
  |
  v
staging deployment
  |
  +--> integration tests
  +--> larger eval suite
  +--> load test
  |
  v
shadow
  |
  v
canary
  |
  v
full production

Testing Before Release

Test categories:

Test Purpose
unit tests application logic
prompt tests prompt renders correctly
schema tests tool and JSON outputs validate
offline evals task quality
safety evals harmful or disallowed behavior
regression evals known historical failures
cost tests token and route budget
load tests latency and throughput
chaos tests provider failure and fallback

Regression evals are especially valuable. Every production failure should become a test case.

Deployment Manifests in CI

CI should validate manifests:

  • model reference exists
  • digest is pinned
  • tokenizer is attached
  • prompt version exists
  • eval suite passed
  • rollback target exists
  • resource request is valid
  • route policy is allowed
  • tenant restrictions are respected

This prevents a large class of avoidable release failures.

Release Approval

Not every change needs the same approval.

Change Approval level
typo in non-production prompt low
prompt change for production support bot medium
model version change high
provider migration high
safety policy change high
gateway quota policy high
observability-only change medium

Use risk-based approval. Do not make every change painful, but do not let high-risk changes sneak through.

Key Takeaways

  • AI CI/CD must test prompts, models, tools, policies, cost, and latency.
  • Behavior changes can happen outside application code.
  • Deployment manifests should be validated automatically.
  • Production failures should become regression evals.

Common Mistakes

  • Running only unit tests before model release.
  • Not testing prompt rendering.
  • Allowing unpinned model aliases.
  • Skipping load tests for engine changes.
  • Treating eval failures as warnings forever.

Production Insights

The most mature AI teams turn incidents into datasets. Every bad answer, invalid tool call, unsafe response, and expensive runaway request becomes a future gate.

Industry Best Practices

  • Store prompts and policies in version control.
  • Require evals for model and prompt changes.
  • Validate manifests in CI.
  • Keep rollback targets pinned.
  • Automate shadow and canary setup from release config.

11. Self-Hosted Serving Selection

Intuition

Choosing a serving engine is part of release engineering because the engine defines deployment behavior.

The same model served through llama.cpp, Ollama, TGI, vLLM, SGLang, or TensorRT-LLM can have different latency, throughput, observability, quantization support, hardware requirements, and rollout risk.

The engine is not an implementation detail. It is part of the production contract.

The Core Problem

Teams often choose serving engines based on familiarity:

  • “Ollama works on my laptop.”
  • “Hugging Face TGI is what we used before.”
  • “vLLM is popular.”
  • “TensorRT-LLM is fastest.”

All can be true in different contexts. The right selection depends on hardware, scale, and workload.

Engine Map

From the Phase 17 source:

Engine Best for Production note
llama.cpp CPU, edge, minimal dependencies strongest CPU/local path
Ollama dev laptops, one-command local use great for dev, not shared production default
TGI Hugging Face ecosystem, existing deployments maintenance-mode risk for new projects
vLLM general-purpose production serving strong default
SGLang agentic, prefix-heavy, multi-turn serving strong for cache-heavy workloads
TensorRT-LLM NVIDIA-optimized high throughput best when NVIDIA stack is the constraint

Decision Tree

CPU only?
  -> llama.cpp

developer laptop?
  -> Ollama

general production on NVIDIA/AMD?
  -> vLLM

agentic multi-turn or heavy prefix reuse?
  -> SGLang

Blackwell / NVIDIA throughput-max environment?
  -> TensorRT-LLM

existing TGI deployment?
  -> maintain, evaluate migration

Dev, Staging, Production

A practical pipeline:

dev: Ollama or llama.cpp
staging: same weights, production-like quantization
production: vLLM, SGLang, or TensorRT-LLM

Do not let dev and production drift too far. If developers test a different chat template, quantization format, or context limit locally, staging will catch problems late.

Release Implications

Engine changes require serious rollout:

  • run offline evals again
  • run load tests again
  • compare output formatting
  • compare streaming behavior
  • compare tool-call validity
  • compare cache behavior
  • canary gradually
  • keep rollback engine ready

An engine upgrade can change behavior even with the same model.

Key Takeaways

  • Serving engine selection affects latency, cost, features, and rollout risk.
  • Choose based on hardware first, scale second, workload third.
  • Ollama is excellent for dev, not the default production serving engine.
  • vLLM and SGLang are common production defaults for different workloads.

Common Mistakes

  • Using laptop tooling as production architecture.
  • Choosing an engine without workload-specific load tests.
  • Ignoring hardware compatibility.
  • Treating engine upgrades as safe patch releases.
  • Forgetting observability differences across engines.

Production Insights

The serving engine is where model theory meets hardware reality. A release plan that ignores the engine is incomplete.

Industry Best Practices

  • Standardize supported engines per hardware class.
  • Keep dev/stage/prod artifacts compatible.
  • Benchmark with realistic prompts.
  • Treat engine upgrades as high-risk releases.
  • Maintain rollback paths for engine changes.

12. Incident-Safe Release Engineering

Intuition

Incident-safe release engineering means assuming releases can fail and designing the system so failure is contained.

The goal is not to prevent every bad release. That is impossible. The goal is to detect, limit, and reverse bad releases quickly.

The Release Runbook

A mature LLM release runbook includes:

  1. change summary
  2. affected models, prompts, tools, and routes
  3. offline eval results
  4. load-test results
  5. shadow plan
  6. canary plan
  7. gates and thresholds
  8. rollback target
  9. owner and on-call
  10. communication plan

If you cannot state the rollback target before rollout, do not roll out.

Rollback Strategies

Rollback options:

Strategy Use when
route flip model/prompt policy change
feature flag off product-level AI feature
provider fallback provider outage
previous digest pin model artifact regression
blue-green reversal environment-level issue
quota throttle cost or abuse incident
batch pause offline job causing overload

Rollback should preserve evidence. Do not delete traces or logs during cleanup. You need them for postmortem and regression tests.

Safe Failure Modes

An AI system should degrade safely:

  • use smaller model
  • reduce max output tokens
  • disable non-critical tools
  • queue batch work
  • return template response
  • ask user to retry
  • route to human support
  • stop agent loop

The worst failure mode is an expensive, unsafe, unbounded loop that keeps running.

Post-Release Monitoring

After rollout, monitor:

  • latency percentiles
  • cost per request
  • route distribution
  • refusal rate
  • error rate
  • output length
  • tool failures
  • cache hit rate
  • user feedback
  • safety flags
  • tenant complaints

Monitor by segment. A release can be fine globally and broken for one language, tenant, model route, or prompt type.

Postmortems and Regression

Every release incident should produce:

  • timeline
  • failed gate
  • missing signal
  • blast radius
  • rollback time
  • root cause
  • prevention change
  • new eval or load test

The last item is the most important. If an incident does not become a future gate, you are renting the same failure again.

Key Takeaways

  • Assume AI releases can fail.
  • Rollback target must be known before rollout.
  • Safe degradation is part of architecture.
  • Incidents should become evals, tests, and policy improvements.

Common Mistakes

  • Rolling out without an owner watching metrics.
  • Discovering rollback steps during the incident.
  • Monitoring only global averages.
  • Forgetting to pause batch jobs during capacity incidents.
  • Closing postmortems without adding regression coverage.

Production Insights

Fast rollback is more important than perfect prediction. You will not catch every behavior change offline. But you can design a release system that limits damage when reality disagrees with your evals.

Industry Best Practices

  • Define rollback before rollout.
  • Keep release ownership explicit.
  • Automate hard-gate rollback where possible.
  • Monitor by tenant, region, model, and route.
  • Convert incidents into regression tests.

Putting It Together: A Full LLM Release Workflow

Here is a concrete workflow for releasing a new support assistant model.

Step 1: Register the Candidate

model_id: support-assistant
version: 2026-05-09.1
weights_digest: sha256:...
tokenizer_digest: sha256:...
prompt_version: support-system-v18
engine: vllm-0.15.1
quantization: fp8
rollback: support-assistant:2026-04-18.3

Step 2: Run Offline Gates

Run:

  • support QA evals
  • refusal evals
  • tool-call schema tests
  • hallucination checks
  • prompt injection tests
  • cost simulation
  • output-length comparison

Block release if the candidate fails critical regressions.

Step 3: Load Test

Use realistic prompt distributions:

  • short support questions
  • long account histories
  • multilingual requests
  • tool-heavy workflows
  • cacheable repeated prefixes
  • non-cacheable random prompts

Gate on TTFT, TPOT, error rate, queue latency, and cost.

Step 4: Shadow

Send 5-10% of production requests to the candidate as shadow traffic.

Compare:

  • output length
  • cost per request
  • latency
  • refusal rate
  • tool validity
  • judge score sample
  • safety flags

Do not expose users yet.

Step 5: Canary

Roll out:

1% -> 10% -> 25% -> 50% -> 75% -> 100%

At each step, check:

  • P99 TTFT
  • P99 TPOT
  • cost/request
  • user feedback
  • refusal rate
  • tool errors
  • output length
  • support tickets

If a hard gate fails, rollback by route config.

Step 6: A/B If Product Impact Is Unclear

If the model is stable but behavior is meaningfully different, run an A/B test.

Primary metric:

resolved support conversations per user

Guardrails:

  • cost per resolved conversation
  • latency
  • escalation to human
  • thumbs-down
  • safety flags

Step 7: Full Rollout and Monitor

After full rollout, keep heightened monitoring for at least one business cycle. Many enterprise failures appear only when specific customers or scheduled jobs run.

Step 8: Close the Release

Archive:

  • manifest
  • eval results
  • load-test results
  • canary metrics
  • A/B decision
  • final model reference
  • rollback target

This creates the audit trail for future debugging.

Chapter Summary

Deployment is where LLM systems become real systems.

An AI gateway centralizes provider access, authentication, routing, retries, rate limits, quotas, secrets, observability, and guardrails. It prevents every application from reinventing model infrastructure badly.

Authentication, rate limits, and quotas must be token-aware and cost-aware. LLM requests have wildly different cost profiles, and agents can multiply calls inside loops.

Provider abstraction gives product teams a stable interface while platform teams manage routing, fallback, policy, and provider changes. But the abstraction must not erase important model capabilities.

Model registries and deployment manifests make releases reproducible. A deployable model is not only weights. It includes tokenizer, prompt, tool schemas, quantization, engine config, routing, safety, evals, and rollback target.

Shadow traffic tests candidates on real production distribution without user impact. Canary rollout exposes users gradually with gates for latency, cost, errors, refusals, output length, tool validity, and feedback. Blue-green deployment helps with large environment changes. A/B testing tells you whether users actually prefer the new behavior.

Load testing for LLMs must use realistic prompt distributions and token-level streaming metrics. A repeated-prompt loop can produce dangerously false confidence.

AI CI/CD must validate prompts, tools, manifests, evals, safety, cost, and performance, not only application code. Serving engine selection is part of release engineering because the engine changes latency, features, cache behavior, and rollout risk.

The central rule is simple:

Never ship an LLM change unless you can observe it, limit it, and roll it back.

That rule is the difference between a demo and a production AI system.

Chapter 4 - SRE, Security, Compliance, and FinOps for AI

The model is live. The rollout passed. The dashboard is green. The launch message went out.

Now the real work starts.

Production AI systems do not fail only when the model server crashes. They fail when a provider returns 429s during peak traffic. They fail when a long-context request triggers a KV cache storm. They fail when a leaked API key burns through the monthly budget. They fail when a prompt accidentally sends protected health information to a provider without a Business Associate Agreement. They fail when the audit log cannot answer who saw which data. They fail when a tenant’s agent loops for 400 tool calls overnight. They fail when the product is growing and nobody can say whether the largest customer is profitable.

That is why Volume 3 cannot stop at serving and deployment. Running AI in production requires four operational disciplines:

  • Site Reliability Engineering (SRE): keep the system available, observable, and recoverable.
  • Security: protect credentials, prompts, data, networks, and audit trails.
  • Compliance: prove the system satisfies legal, customer, and governance requirements.
  • FinOps: control unit economics, cost attribution, budgets, and spend anomalies.

These are not separate departments bolted onto the end. They are design constraints. A system that cannot be monitored is not production-ready. A system that cannot rotate credentials is not secure. A system that cannot produce audit evidence is not enterprise-ready. A system that cannot attribute spend is not financially manageable.

This chapter covers the final part of Phase 17: how to operate production AI systems after they are deployed.

The Operations Stack

Simple Intuition

Think of production AI like a power plant.

Serving infrastructure is the machinery. Release engineering is how you safely bring a new turbine online. SRE is the control room. Security is the fence, access badge system, and emergency shutdown procedure. Compliance is the inspection record. FinOps is the fuel bill and efficiency report.

If any one of those is missing, the plant may run for a while, but it is not professionally operated.

The Production Control Loop

Production AI operations should work as a loop:

observe
  |
  v
detect
  |
  v
triage
  |
  v
mitigate
  |
  v
explain
  |
  v
prevent recurrence

For AI systems, the loop includes both infrastructure and behavior.

infrastructure signals:
  latency
  error rate
  GPU memory
  queue depth
  provider health
  cache hit rate

behavior signals:
  refusal rate
  hallucination reports
  prompt injection flags
  tool failure rate
  output length drift
  eval score drift

business signals:
  cost per tenant
  cost per task
  budget burn
  renewal profitability
  abuse patterns

The best AI platform teams do not treat these as separate worlds. A KV cache incident can become a reliability incident, a cost incident, and a customer incident at the same time.

1. SRE for AI Systems

Intuition

SRE is the discipline of making services reliable by using software engineering, measurement, and operational practice.

For AI systems, SRE expands. You are not only keeping an HTTP service alive. You are keeping an inference workflow useful, affordable, safe, and recoverable.

A normal service can be healthy if it returns HTTP 200 quickly. An LLM service can return HTTP 200 while producing invalid JSON, hallucinating, leaking data, refusing normal requests, or spending too much money. AI SRE must care about those failures too.

The Core Problem

The classic on-call story is familiar:

03:00 - pager fires
03:03 - engineer opens dashboard
03:08 - engineer checks logs
03:15 - engineer checks recent deploys
03:25 - engineer finds root cause
03:30 - mitigation starts

In LLM systems, the root cause may be buried across:

  • gateway retries
  • provider rate limits
  • GPU out-of-memory errors
  • KV cache evictions
  • prompt length distribution
  • routing policy changes
  • model version changes
  • batch jobs competing with interactive traffic
  • semantic cache returning stale outputs
  • tool-call loops

This investigation is repetitive. Much of it can be assisted by AI grounded in operational data.

AI SRE Architecture

The Phase 17 source describes a 2026 pattern: multi-agent incident triage with human approval.

incident
  |
  v
supervisor agent
  |
  +--> log agent
  +--> metric agent
  +--> trace agent
  +--> runbook agent
  +--> deploy-history agent
  |
  v
hypotheses + evidence
  |
  v
human on-call approval
  |
  v
safe action

The supervisor does not magically fix production. It coordinates investigation:

  • ask the log agent for error clusters
  • ask the metric agent for latency, memory, and queue anomalies
  • ask the trace agent for slow-path examples
  • ask the runbook agent for known mitigations
  • ask the deploy-history agent what changed

The output should look like an incident briefing:

hypothesis:
  vLLM workers are OOMing from a long-context traffic spike.

evidence:
  GPU memory rose from 71 GB to 79 GB on H100 workers.
  KV cache preemption count increased 12x.
  P99 TTFT rose after tenant acme sent 80K-token requests.
  No deploy occurred in the last 6 hours.

suggested mitigation:
  temporarily route tenant acme long-context traffic to batch lane,
  reduce max concurrent long-context requests,
  scale GPU pool by 2 workers.

human decision required:
  approve mitigation?

That is useful. It saves the first 20 minutes of incident archaeology.

Auto-Remediation Scope

Auto-remediation means the system takes action automatically.

In AI SRE, keep the action set narrow.

Safe or semi-safe actions:

  • restart a known-bad pod
  • roll back a specific deployment
  • disable a canary
  • scale a pool within pre-approved bounds
  • tighten a tenant rate limit
  • pause a batch queue
  • fail over to a pre-approved provider

Unsafe actions:

  • change IAM policy
  • modify database state
  • deploy unreviewed code
  • change model behavior broadly
  • alter customer data
  • reconfigure networking without review
  • rewrite service topology

The rule is simple:

AI can propose broad actions.
AI should only execute narrow pre-approved actions.

Anyone selling fully autonomous production remediation for complex systems is overselling. The safe frontier grows over time, but judgment calls still need humans.

Operational Memory

Teams forget. People leave. Incidents repeat.

Operational memory stores runbooks, incident timelines, postmortems, dashboards, known failure modes, and previous mitigations in a retrievable system.

new incident
  |
  v
retrieve similar incidents
  |
  v
compare symptoms
  |
  v
recommend likely causes

This is where retrieval-augmented generation is boring and valuable. The agent does not need to invent. It needs to retrieve the last time the same symptom happened.

Runbooks should become structured documents, not loose wiki pages.

Template:

title:
symptoms:
customer impact:
dashboards:
queries:
likely causes:
verification steps:
safe mitigations:
rollback:
escalation:
postmortem links:

Structured runbooks retrieve better and are easier for humans to follow under pressure.

Adversarial Incident Analysis

The source material describes an adversarial pattern: two models independently analyze the same incident. If they agree, confidence rises. If they disagree, show both hypotheses.

model A: KV cache exhaustion from long prompts
model B: provider 429 retry storm

supervisor:
  disagreement detected
  present both evidence sets to human

This is not perfect, but it is useful. It reduces the chance that one confident model hallucination becomes the incident narrative.

Pre-Incident Prediction

Predictive detection tries to warn before the outage.

Signals may include:

  • rising GPU temperature
  • increasing queue depth
  • higher KV eviction rate
  • provider error-rate drift
  • abnormal token velocity
  • growing memory fragmentation
  • retry rate slope
  • cache hit-rate collapse

A warning without an action plan is just a dashboard. The important question is:

If we predict failure in 10 minutes, what do we do now?

Possible actions:

  • pre-scale GPU pool
  • drain hot replicas
  • pause batch jobs
  • tighten rate limits
  • switch providers
  • page on-call before users notice

Key Takeaways

  • AI SRE uses LLMs grounded in logs, metrics, traces, runbooks, and topology.
  • Multi-agent triage is useful for investigation, not magical autonomy.
  • Auto-remediation should be narrow and pre-approved.
  • Operational memory turns previous incidents into future speed.

Common Mistakes

  • Letting an AI agent execute broad production changes.
  • Feeding agents unstructured, stale runbooks.
  • Treating HTTP 200 as success for LLM systems.
  • Ignoring cost and behavior signals during incidents.
  • Failing to convert incidents into runbook updates.

Production Insights

The best first use of AI in SRE is not autonomous repair. It is faster investigation: summarize symptoms, find correlated changes, retrieve similar incidents, and prepare a clear hypothesis for the human on-call.

Industry Best Practices

  • Keep AI SRE read-only at first.
  • Add narrow actions only after repeated human-approved success.
  • Version runbooks as code.
  • Attach every recommendation to evidence.
  • Store postmortems in the retrieval corpus.
  • Require human approval for customer-impacting changes.

2. SLIs, SLOs, Error Budgets, and Runbooks

Intuition

You cannot operate what you have not defined.

An SLI, or service-level indicator, is a measurement. An SLO, or service-level objective, is the target for that measurement. An error budget is the amount of failure you are allowed before you slow down risky changes.

For AI systems, you need service SLOs and behavior SLOs.

The Core Problem

Traditional SLO:

99.9% of API requests return HTTP 2xx under 500 ms.

LLM service SLO:

99% of chat requests produce first token under 800 ms.
99% of output tokens stream with TPOT under 25 ms.
99.9% of requests complete without gateway/provider error.
Tool-call JSON validity stays above 99.5%.
Cost per resolved ticket stays below target.
False refusal rate stays below threshold.

The second set is harder but more honest.

AI SLIs

Category SLIs
availability success rate, 5xx rate, timeout rate
latency TTFT, TPOT, E2E, queue time
capacity requests/sec, tokens/sec, GPU utilization
memory GPU memory, KV cache usage, eviction rate
quality eval score sample, user rating, tool validity
safety jailbreak flags, PII flags, refusal correctness
cost cost/request, cost/task, tenant spend velocity
routing fallback rate, provider error rate, route distribution

An SLO should be specific enough to page on.

Bad:

The assistant should be fast.

Good:

P99 TTFT for interactive support chat must be <= 900 ms over 15-minute windows, excluding planned provider incidents tagged in the incident log.

Error Budgets

An error budget turns reliability into a release decision.

If the service promises 99.9% availability, it has about 0.1% allowable failure over the measurement window. If burn rate is too high, you pause risky rollouts and focus on reliability.

AI teams should add budget policies for:

  • downtime
  • latency SLO miss
  • cost anomaly
  • safety incident
  • invalid tool-call rate

Example:

If daily error-budget burn exceeds 2x expected, pause canaries and chaos experiments.

That exact kind of guardrail appears in the chaos engineering lesson.

Runbooks as Code

Runbooks should live in version control.

Why?

  • reviewable
  • searchable
  • testable
  • linkable from alerts
  • usable by AI SRE retrieval
  • updated in postmortems

Runbook sections:

symptom:
impact:
first checks:
dashboard links:
log queries:
trace queries:
known causes:
safe mitigations:
unsafe actions:
rollback:
escalation:
customer communication:

Key Takeaways

  • AI operations need infrastructure and behavior SLOs.
  • Error budgets should control release and chaos activity.
  • Runbooks should be structured and versioned.
  • Good SLOs are concrete enough to trigger action.

Common Mistakes

  • Using uptime-only SLOs for LLM products.
  • Ignoring token latency.
  • Writing runbooks as vague wiki pages.
  • Letting canaries continue while error budget burns.
  • Not linking alerts to runbooks.

Production Insights

AI systems need SLOs that reflect user experience and business risk. A system that is technically up but returns invalid tool calls is down for the workflow that depends on those tools.

Industry Best Practices

  • Define SLOs per workload class.
  • Include TTFT, TPOT, success, and tool validity.
  • Use burn-rate alerts.
  • Pause risky changes when error budget is low.
  • Attach runbooks to every critical alert.

3. Chaos Engineering for LLM Production

Intuition

Chaos engineering is controlled failure injection.

You intentionally break small parts of the system to learn whether the whole system survives. The point is not drama. The point is evidence.

For LLM systems, chaos engineering is especially important because many failures do not appear in unit tests:

  • provider outage
  • malformed prompt
  • KV cache eviction storm
  • gateway retry amplification
  • memory overload
  • GPU node loss
  • tokenizer slowdown
  • semantic cache corruption

If you never test these, users become the experiment.

Prerequisites

Do not run chaos experiments in production until you have:

  1. SLIs and SLOs
  2. observability
  3. automated rollback
  4. runbooks
  5. on-call ownership

Missing any of these turns chaos engineering into an outage generator.

Chaos Architecture

control plane
  experiment scheduler
  scenario definitions

target plane
  pods
  nodes
  gateways
  providers
  caches
  data stores

safety plane
  blast-radius limits
  abort rules
  error-budget gates
  kill switch

observability plane
  metrics
  logs
  traces
  experiment tags

feedback loop
  postmortem
  runbook update
  SLO adjustment
  code/config fix

The safety plane is mandatory. Every experiment needs an abort condition.

Example:

abort if:
  P99 TTFT > 2x baseline for 5 minutes
  5xx rate > 2%
  daily error-budget burn > 2x expected
  on-call manually stops experiment

LLM-Specific Chaos Experiments

Provider outage

Inject 100% 429s or 5xxs from the primary provider.

Question:

Does the gateway fall back without retry storm?

Watch:

  • retry count
  • fallback latency
  • cost change
  • user-visible error rate
  • provider-specific output differences

GPU memory overload

Send long-context requests at high concurrency.

Question:

Does the serving stack shed load, queue safely, or crash?

Watch:

  • GPU memory
  • KV cache usage
  • preemption rate
  • queue latency
  • worker restarts

KV cache eviction storm

Force cache pressure by mixing long prompts, many sessions, and limited block budget.

Question:

Does cache eviction cause re-prefill cascades?

Watch:

  • cache hit rate
  • prefill latency
  • TTFT
  • compute saturation
  • LMCache or external cache behavior

Malformed prompt

Inject prompts with unusual Unicode, deeply nested structures, or extremely long tokens.

Question:

Can one input stall tokenization or parsing?

Watch:

  • tokenizer latency
  • CPU usage
  • request timeout
  • worker health
  • input validation logs

Batch queue overload

Flood batch jobs while interactive traffic runs.

Question:

Are batch and interactive lanes isolated?

Watch:

  • interactive TTFT
  • GPU allocation
  • queue starvation
  • batch pause behavior

Cadence

A practical cadence:

Frequency Activity
weekly small staging or limited canary experiment
monthly game day with cross-team participation
quarterly resilience audit and dependency map update

Game days are rehearsals. You want engineers to practice failover before the real outage.

Tooling

Common chaos tools:

  • LitmusChaos
  • Chaos Mesh
  • Harness Chaos Engineering
  • Gremlin
  • AWS Fault Injection Service
  • Azure Chaos Studio

For Kubernetes-based LLM stacks, LitmusChaos and Chaos Mesh are natural starting points. Managed cloud tools help when the target is cloud infrastructure. Commercial tools may help with governance, reporting, and blast-radius control.

Key Takeaways

  • Chaos engineering discovers failure modes before users do.
  • LLM systems need LLM-specific experiments, not only pod kills.
  • The safety plane is mandatory.
  • Findings must feed back into SLOs, runbooks, and architecture.

Common Mistakes

  • Running chaos without SLOs.
  • Running production experiments without rollback.
  • Forgetting to tag experiment traffic.
  • Testing only network failures, not prompt and KV failures.
  • Never updating runbooks after experiments.

Production Insights

The first valuable chaos test is often boring: simulate provider 429s and verify fallback. Many teams discover their fallback exists in code but fails under real retry and latency conditions.

Industry Best Practices

  • Start in staging, then small production blast radius.
  • Use abort rules tied to error-budget burn.
  • Tag all experiment-induced errors.
  • Run monthly game days.
  • Add every finding to a runbook or backlog item.

4. Security: Secrets, Keys, and Credential Hygiene

Intuition

LLM credentials are production money keys.

A leaked provider key can expose customer data, burn budget, or let an attacker impersonate your product. A leaked CI/CD credential can expose all environment variables in downstream deployments. A permissive network policy can turn an LLM pod into a data exfiltration path.

Security starts with a simple rule:

No application should own long-lived AI provider secrets directly.

The Core Problem

Secret sprawl happens quietly:

  • API keys in .env files
  • keys in CI/CD variables
  • keys pasted into Slack
  • keys copied into spreadsheets
  • keys stored in Kubernetes secrets forever
  • keys committed to git history
  • keys shared across services

When rotation becomes painful, teams stop rotating. When nobody knows where a key is used, incidents become long and messy.

Vault-Centered Architecture

Use a centralized secret store:

  • HashiCorp Vault
  • AWS Secrets Manager
  • Azure Key Vault
  • Google Secret Manager

Architecture:

application
  |
  v
AI gateway
  |
  v
vault / secret manager
  |
  v
provider API key

The gateway authenticates to the vault using workload identity, not a static key. The provider credential stays centralized. Rotation happens in the vault. Applications do not redeploy just to pick up a new provider key.

IAM Roles Over Static Keys

For cloud workloads, prefer identity-based access:

  • IAM roles
  • workload identity
  • OIDC for CI/CD
  • short-lived credentials
  • scoped permissions

CI/CD deserves special attention. Build systems often have access to production secrets. Treat CI/CD identity as production identity.

Rotation Policy

A practical baseline:

rotate provider API keys at least every 90 days
rotate immediately on suspected exposure
rotate CI/CD credentials aggressively
automate rotation where possible
log every rotation event

Rotation should not require a company-wide Slack message. If it does, the architecture is wrong.

Secret Scanning

Run secret scanning on every commit.

Tools include:

  • TruffleHog
  • GitGuardian
  • Gitleaks

If a secret lands in git history:

  1. revoke or rotate the secret
  2. investigate usage
  3. remove from history where appropriate
  4. add detection rule if missed
  5. write a short incident note

Deleting the line from the latest commit is not enough. Git history still has it.

Network Egress Control

LLM services should not reach the entire internet.

Use egress allowlists:

allowed:
  api.openai.com
  api.anthropic.com
  bedrock runtime endpoint
  vault endpoint
  vector database endpoint
  observability collector

blocked:
  everything else

This limits data exfiltration paths. Also control DNS. If pods can resolve and call arbitrary domains, DNS tunneling and strange exfiltration paths become possible.

Key Takeaways

  • Centralize AI provider credentials in a vault.
  • Prefer workload identity and short-lived tokens.
  • Rotate keys regularly and immediately on exposure.
  • Restrict network egress from LLM services.

Common Mistakes

  • Putting provider keys in application config.
  • Sharing one key across many services.
  • Rotating by manual redeploy.
  • Ignoring CI/CD credentials.
  • Allowing unrestricted outbound internet from model services.

Production Insights

The AI gateway pattern is not only about routing. It is a security control. It lets applications call one internal service while the gateway handles provider credentials, audit, policy, and rotation.

Industry Best Practices

  • Scan every commit for secrets.
  • Store secrets in a centralized vault.
  • Use OIDC for CI/CD instead of static deploy keys.
  • Scope provider keys by environment and purpose.
  • Maintain a credential inventory.
  • Test rotation during game days.

5. Prompt Data Security, PII Scrubbing, and Audit Logs

Intuition

LLM security is not only about keys. The prompt is data.

Users paste names, addresses, account numbers, contracts, health details, source code, credentials, and internal strategy into AI systems. If you send that prompt to a model provider, log it in plain text, or expose it in traces, you have a data security problem.

The safe default is:

classify and minimize sensitive data before it leaves your controlled boundary.

PII and PHI

PII means personally identifiable information. Examples:

  • name
  • email
  • phone number
  • address
  • Social Security number
  • account number

PHI means protected health information under HIPAA. It is health-related data tied to an individual.

For regulated workloads, you need to know whether prompts contain PII, PHI, payment data, secrets, or customer confidential data.

Real-Time Redaction

Post-processing is not enough. If you redact after the LLM call, the model provider already received the data.

Real-time inference-layer redaction:

raw prompt
  |
  v
PII/PHI detector
  |
  v
consistent tokenizer / masker
  |
  v
redacted prompt to model
  |
  v
optional reverse mapping

Example:

Raw:
My SSN is 123-45-6789 and my wife's SSN is 123-45-6789.

Redacted:
My SSN is [SSN_A] and my wife's SSN is [SSN_A].

Consistent tokenization matters. The same sensitive value maps to the same placeholder so the model preserves relationships.

Input and Output Guardrails

Input guardrails:

  • PII/PHI detection
  • prompt injection detection
  • forbidden topic checks
  • credential pattern detection
  • tenant policy checks
  • request size limits

Output guardrails:

  • secret leakage detection
  • PII leakage detection
  • unsafe content classification
  • schema validation
  • refusal correctness checks
  • policy compliance checks

Do not rely on the model to self-police everything. Use external controls.

Audit Logs

An audit log is an immutable record of important actions.

For LLM calls, log:

  • timestamp
  • tenant
  • user or service identity
  • model and version
  • prompt template version
  • route decision
  • provider
  • token counts
  • cost
  • cache status
  • guardrail decisions
  • prompt hash
  • response hash
  • trace id

Do not store raw prompts unless you have a clear policy, consent, retention rule, and access control.

Example:

{
  "timestamp": "2026-05-09T10:15:00Z",
  "tenant_id": "tenant_42",
  "user_id": "user_19",
  "model": "support-assistant:2026-05-09.1",
  "provider": "azure-openai",
  "prompt_hash": "sha256:...",
  "response_hash": "sha256:...",
  "input_tokens": 1842,
  "output_tokens": 212,
  "cost_usd": 0.018,
  "guardrails": ["pii_redacted"],
  "trace_id": "abc123"
}

Retention and Access

Audit logs are sensitive. Protect them.

Controls:

  • append-only storage
  • encryption at rest
  • access logging
  • role-based access
  • retention tiers
  • legal hold support
  • deletion workflows where required

For compliance, retention requirements differ. HIPAA can require long retention for certain records. SOC 2 evidence usually needs operated control history. GDPR may require deletion rights. Design retention with legal guidance.

Key Takeaways

  • Prompts and responses are sensitive data.
  • Redact sensitive data before model calls when required.
  • Use consistent tokenization to preserve semantics.
  • Audit logs should prove what happened without overexposing raw content.

Common Mistakes

  • Logging raw prompts by default.
  • Redacting only after model response.
  • Using regex-only PII detection for complex domains.
  • Storing audit logs without access controls.
  • Forgetting output leakage checks.

Production Insights

The safest observability design separates operational metadata from sensitive content. You can debug many incidents with hashes, token counts, route decisions, guardrail flags, and sampled redacted traces.

Industry Best Practices

  • Redact before external provider calls when policy requires.
  • Use both regex and entity recognition for PII detection.
  • Store prompt hashes by default.
  • Limit raw prompt access to approved workflows.
  • Make audit logs append-only.
  • Review retention policy with legal and security.

6. Compliance Frameworks for AI Products

Intuition

Compliance is the ability to prove your system follows required rules.

For enterprise AI, compliance is not optional paperwork. It is part of sales, legal, security, procurement, and risk management. A customer may like your model and still refuse to buy because you cannot produce SOC 2 evidence, a data-processing agreement, a HIPAA BAA, or an AI governance statement.

Compliance turns engineering controls into evidence.

The Core Problem

Modern AI products often touch multiple regimes:

  • SOC 2 for B2B SaaS trust
  • ISO 27001 for security management
  • ISO/IEC 42001 for AI management systems
  • GDPR for EU personal data
  • HIPAA for US healthcare data
  • PCI-DSS for payment data
  • EU AI Act for AI systems in or affecting the EU
  • state-level AI laws such as Colorado’s AI Act

The hard part is not memorizing every framework. The hard part is building controls once and mapping them across frameworks.

Framework Map

Framework What it cares about AI-specific angle
SOC 2 Type II operated security and availability controls enterprise SaaS baseline
ISO 27001 information security management broad security governance
ISO/IEC 42001 AI management system AI risk, governance, transparency
GDPR EU personal data protection lawful basis, data rights, minimization
HIPAA US protected health information BAA required for PHI processors
PCI-DSS payment card data prevent card data entering prompts
EU AI Act AI risk classification and obligations high-risk systems need documentation/logging
Colorado AI Act high-risk AI decision systems impact assessments and consumer rights

EU AI Act

Regulation (EU) 2024/1689 is the EU Artificial Intelligence Act. It entered into force in 2024. The regulation generally applies from 2 August 2026, with some provisions applying earlier and some later. Prohibited practice rules began applying from 2 February 2025. General-purpose AI model obligations apply from 2 August 2025. Certain Article 6(1) obligations apply from 2 August 2027.

The operational point: if you serve EU users, you need a risk classification and evidence plan.

Risk categories:

Risk tier Example Operational meaning
unacceptable banned practices do not build or deploy
high risk employment, credit, education, essential services documentation, logging, risk management
limited risk chatbots, generated-content transparency disclosure and transparency
minimal risk low-risk use lighter obligations

Fines can be serious. The EU AI Act lists administrative fines up to EUR 35 million or 7% of worldwide annual turnover for prohibited AI practices, and up to EUR 15 million or 3% for certain other operator obligations.

This is not legal advice. The engineering action is clear: classify use cases early and keep evidence.

GDPR

GDPR cares about personal data.

For LLM systems, key engineering concerns:

  • avoid sending unnecessary personal data
  • redact before inference where required
  • record lawful basis
  • support data subject rights
  • define retention
  • control subprocessors
  • maintain processing records
  • protect logs and traces

The dangerous pattern is prompt hoarding: storing raw prompts forever because “they might help improve the model.” That creates privacy and retention risk.

HIPAA

HIPAA applies to protected health information in covered healthcare contexts.

If your system sends PHI to an external AI provider, you need a Business Associate Agreement with that provider. No BAA, no PHI. That rule should be enforced in routing policy, not left to developer memory.

Example:

if data_classification == PHI:
  allowed_providers = providers_with_signed_BAA
  log_raw_prompt = false
  require_region = approved_healthcare_region

SOC 2 Type II

SOC 2 Type I says controls are designed at a point in time. SOC 2 Type II says controls operated over a period, often 6-12 months.

For AI products, SOC 2 evidence may include:

  • access reviews
  • change management records
  • incident response tests
  • vendor risk reviews
  • audit logs
  • key rotation evidence
  • backup and recovery tests
  • monitoring and alerting evidence
  • vulnerability management

Your model release records from Chapter 3 become compliance evidence.

ISO/IEC 42001

ISO/IEC 42001:2023 is an AI management system standard. It specifies requirements for establishing, implementing, maintaining, and improving an Artificial Intelligence Management System.

In practical terms, it asks:

  • do you have AI governance?
  • do you manage AI risks?
  • do you document AI system lifecycle decisions?
  • do you define roles and responsibilities?
  • do you monitor and improve?
  • do you handle transparency and accountability?

It pairs naturally with ISO 27001. ISO 27001 governs information security. ISO 42001 governs AI management.

Cross-Framework Mapping

One engineering control can satisfy multiple frameworks.

Control Frameworks it supports
access control SOC 2, ISO 27001, HIPAA, GDPR
audit logging SOC 2, HIPAA, EU AI Act, ISO 42001
change management SOC 2, ISO 27001, ISO 42001
data minimization GDPR, HIPAA, ISO 42001
incident response SOC 2, ISO 27001, GDPR, HIPAA
vendor risk management SOC 2, GDPR, HIPAA
model risk assessment EU AI Act, ISO 42001, Colorado AI Act

The mature move is to maintain a compliance matrix:

control -> evidence -> owner -> frameworks -> review cadence

Key Takeaways

  • Compliance is evidence that controls exist and operate.
  • AI products often need multiple frameworks at once.
  • EU AI Act and ISO 42001 add AI-specific governance expectations.
  • Build controls once and map them across frameworks.

Common Mistakes

  • Treating compliance as a sales document only.
  • Starting SOC 2 Type II after the customer asks.
  • Sending PHI to providers without a BAA.
  • Logging raw prompts without retention policy.
  • Ignoring AI risk classification until procurement.

Production Insights

Compliance gets much easier when engineering artifacts are structured: deployment manifests, model registry records, audit logs, incident reports, access reviews, and eval results. The same discipline that helps operations helps audits.

Industry Best Practices

  • Maintain a framework-to-control matrix.
  • Classify AI use cases by risk.
  • Keep evidence as part of normal engineering workflow.
  • Use real-time redaction where required.
  • Review provider contracts and subprocessors.
  • Involve legal early for regulated data.

7. Data Residency, Enterprise Audit, and Governance

Intuition

Data residency means data must stay in an approved geography.

Enterprise audit means you must prove what happened.

Governance means decisions have owners, policies, evidence, and review cycles.

Together, these determine whether large customers can trust your AI platform.

The Core Problem

Enterprise AI customers ask questions like:

  • Which region processed our prompts?
  • Which provider saw our data?
  • Were prompts used for training?
  • Who can access logs?
  • How long are traces retained?
  • Can you delete our data?
  • Can you prove a model version?
  • Can you show who changed a prompt?
  • Can you restrict our traffic to EU-only providers?

If the answer requires manually searching five systems, you are not ready.

Residency-Aware Architecture

tenant policy
  |
  v
region allowlist
  |
  v
gateway routing
  |
  v
approved provider/cluster
  |
  v
region-scoped logs and cache

Residency applies beyond model calls:

  • prompts
  • responses
  • logs
  • traces
  • embeddings
  • vector indexes
  • caches
  • batch files
  • audit logs
  • backups

A common failure is routing inference correctly but storing traces in the wrong region.

Enterprise Audit Requirements

Audit evidence should answer:

who did what,
to which model,
with which data class,
in which region,
under which policy,
at what time,
with what result.

This requires joining:

  • identity
  • tenant policy
  • gateway trace
  • model registry
  • deployment manifest
  • audit log
  • cost record
  • incident record if applicable

Governance Board Without Theater

Some AI governance programs become meeting-heavy and engineering-light. Avoid that.

Good governance asks concrete questions:

  • Is this use case high risk?
  • What data classes are involved?
  • Which providers are allowed?
  • What evals are required?
  • What monitoring is required?
  • What is the rollback plan?
  • Who owns incidents?
  • What evidence proves control operation?

Governance should produce decisions that become config, tests, and manifests.

Key Takeaways

  • Data residency must apply to logs, caches, embeddings, and backups, not only inference.
  • Enterprise audit requires joinable records.
  • Governance decisions should become enforceable policy.
  • Tenant policy belongs in the gateway and routing layer.

Common Mistakes

  • Routing prompts regionally but exporting traces globally.
  • Treating embeddings as non-sensitive.
  • Storing cache data outside residency boundary.
  • Keeping governance decisions in slide decks only.
  • Not linking model versions to audit records.

Production Insights

Enterprise AI trust is mostly about control and proof. Customers know models are imperfect. What they need is evidence that you know where their data went, what system touched it, and how to contain failures.

Industry Best Practices

  • Make residency a hard routing constraint.
  • Keep region-scoped observability storage.
  • Treat embeddings and caches as customer data.
  • Attach governance approvals to model releases.
  • Run periodic audit drills.

8. FinOps for LLMs

Intuition

FinOps is financial operations for cloud and infrastructure spend.

Traditional cloud FinOps tracks resources: virtual machines, databases, buckets, clusters. LLM FinOps tracks transactions: prompts, tokens, model calls, tool calls, cache hits, batch jobs, and agent loops.

That difference matters. A GPU node can be tagged. A model call must be attributed at the moment it is created.

If you do not stamp cost metadata into every request, you will not recover accurate attribution later.

The Core Problem

Your provider bill says:

$40,000 this month

But you need to know:

  • which tenant spent it
  • which user triggered it
  • which product feature drove it
  • which model route was used
  • how much was prompt vs memory vs tool vs response
  • how much caching saved
  • which jobs should have used batch
  • whether the tenant is profitable

Without attribution, cost optimization becomes guesswork.

Three Attribution Dimensions

Instrument these from day one:

Dimension Field Why it matters
per-user user_id abuse, seat pricing, power users
per-task task_id, route product feature economics
per-tenant tenant_id customer profitability

Add:

  • environment
  • model id
  • provider
  • region
  • trace id
  • cache status
  • batch status
  • prompt version
  • route reason

Four Token Layers

The source lesson splits token cost into four layers:

Layer Example Optimization lever
prompt system prompt, user input prompt compaction, prefix cache
tool tool results fed back tool result summarization
memory conversation history, retrieved docs memory pruning, retrieval quality
response generated answer max token control, style tuning

If you bucket all tokens together, you cannot optimize intelligently.

Example:

tenant acme support assistant:
  prompt tokens: 45%
  tool tokens: 25%
  memory tokens: 20%
  response tokens: 10%

For that tenant, shortening responses will not fix the main cost. You need prompt/memory/tool optimization.

Attribution Architecture

request created
  |
  v
stamp metadata:
  tenant_id, user_id, task_id, route, trace_id
  |
  v
gateway / model call
  |
  v
token counts and cost emitted
  |
  v
telemetry joiner
  |
  v
billing and analytics tables

The telemetry joiner connects traces to billing records using trace ids. This is more accurate than trying to reconcile provider invoices after the fact.

Example cost event:

{
  "trace_id": "abc123",
  "tenant_id": "tenant_7",
  "user_id": "user_42",
  "task_id": "ticket_resolution",
  "route": "small_model_first",
  "model": "medium-support",
  "prompt_tokens": 1800,
  "tool_tokens": 600,
  "memory_tokens": 400,
  "response_tokens": 150,
  "cached_input_tokens": 1200,
  "batch": false,
  "cost_usd": 0.0135
}

Unit Economics

Do not optimize only dollars per million tokens. That is vendor accounting.

Product metrics:

  • cost per resolved ticket
  • cost per generated report
  • cost per successful agent task
  • cost per qualified lead
  • cost per code review
  • cost per user session

The question is not:

How cheap are tokens?

The question is:

How much does it cost to produce the customer outcome?

Enforcement Ladder

The Phase 17 lesson gives a practical ladder:

1. Rate limits

Set per-tenant rate limits at 2-3x expected peak.

Return clear 429 responses with Retry-After.

2. Daily spend caps

Set daily spend caps around 1.5-3x the contracted or expected ceiling.

When breached:

  • alert customer success
  • tighten rate limits
  • notify tenant admin
  • investigate task mix

3. Kill switch

If spend is abnormal, pause automatically.

Example:

if tenant spend z-score > 4:
  pause tenant high-cost routes
  page on-call
  notify customer success

The point is not to punish customers. The point is to stop runaway loops, key abuse, and accidental cost explosions.

Cost Optimization Stack

Cost levers compound:

prompt cache
  + semantic cache
  + batch API
  + model routing
  + quantization
  + output limits
  + memory pruning
  + tool result compression

Optimization examples:

Symptom Likely fix
high repeated prompt cost prefix caching
many similar FAQ requests semantic cache
offline workload expensive batch API
easy tasks using frontier model model routing
response tokens too high max token and style control
memory tokens exploding conversation summarization
tool tokens huge compress tool outputs
tenant spend spike quota and kill switch

Key Takeaways

  • LLM FinOps tracks transactions, not only resources.
  • Attribute cost by user, task, and tenant from day one.
  • Split token cost into prompt, tool, memory, and response layers.
  • Optimize cost per product outcome, not only cost per token.

Common Mistakes

  • Trying to tag costs retroactively.
  • Tracking provider bill without tenant attribution.
  • Ignoring tool and memory tokens.
  • Letting agents run without spend caps.
  • Optimizing token price while product unit cost stays high.

Production Insights

The most expensive LLM feature is often not the one with the biggest model. It is the one with uncontrolled context growth, tool loops, or no cache. FinOps needs traces, not just invoices.

Industry Best Practices

  • Emit cost events for every model call.
  • Join traces to billing by trace id.
  • Build per-tenant and per-task dashboards.
  • Define kill switches before launch.
  • Review top cost drivers weekly.
  • Tie pricing to product unit economics.

9. Capacity Planning and Production Readiness Reviews

Intuition

Capacity planning asks:

Do we have enough serving capacity for expected demand, failure, and growth?

Production readiness asks:

Are we operationally prepared to run this system safely?

In AI systems, both questions include cost, security, compliance, and reliability.

Capacity Planning for LLMs

Plan around:

  • request rate
  • input token distribution
  • output token distribution
  • concurrency
  • TTFT SLO
  • TPOT SLO
  • cache hit rate
  • batch workload
  • regional routing
  • failover capacity
  • GPU availability
  • provider quotas

Capacity is not just average RPS. A tenant can send fewer requests but more tokens. Long-context traffic can dominate GPU memory. A batch job can starve interactive serving if lanes are not isolated.

Readiness Checklist

Before launch, require:

Area Questions
SRE SLOs, alerts, runbooks, on-call
security vault, rotation, egress, secret scanning
compliance data classification, provider contracts, audit logs
FinOps attribution, budgets, dashboards, kill switches
release rollback, canary, shadow, manifest
load realistic load test, spike test, soak if needed
safety guardrails, evals, incident process
support customer communication and escalation

This checklist should be a gate. If the answer is “we will add observability later,” the system is not ready.

Failure Mode Table

Failure Detection Mitigation
provider outage provider error rate fallback or degrade
GPU OOM worker restart, memory alert reduce concurrency, scale, route
cost spike spend velocity alert rate limit, pause tenant
leaked key secret scanner, anomaly rotate, revoke, investigate
PHI sent without BAA audit/policy violation stop route, incident response
cache storm hit-rate drop, TTFT rise reduce load, external cache
bad model rollout canary gate rollback route
prompt injection guardrail flag block, sanitize, update eval

Key Takeaways

  • Capacity planning for LLMs is token-aware and memory-aware.
  • Production readiness must include SRE, security, compliance, and FinOps.
  • Readiness reviews should block launches when critical controls are missing.
  • Failure modes should have detection and mitigation before launch.

Common Mistakes

  • Planning only by request count.
  • Forgetting provider quota limits.
  • Launching without cost attribution.
  • Treating security review as optional.
  • Not testing failover before launch.

Production Insights

The readiness review is where engineering optimism meets operational reality. It is cheaper to delay a launch than to discover during launch week that rollback, audit logs, and spend caps do not exist.

Industry Best Practices

  • Make production readiness a standard launch gate.
  • Use token-based capacity models.
  • Include provider quota and regional failover.
  • Require dashboards before launch.
  • Test rollback and key rotation.
  • Review cost model with finance or product leadership.

10. Common Production Playbooks

Intuition

A playbook is a pre-decided response to a known class of incident.

During an outage, the team is tired, the clock is running, and customers are waiting. That is the worst time to invent policy. Playbooks move decisions earlier, when everyone is calm.

For production AI systems, the most important playbooks are not exotic. They are the same few failures that keep coming back:

  • provider outage
  • GPU worker failure
  • cost spike
  • leaked key
  • sensitive data exposure
  • bad model rollout
  • prompt injection wave
  • agent loop

Each playbook should answer:

How do we detect it?
How bad is it?
Who owns it?
What do we do first?
What is safe to automate?
When do we escalate?
How do we close the loop?

Provider Outage Playbook

Provider outages are common enough that they should never surprise you.

Detection:

  • provider 5xx rate rises
  • 429 rate rises
  • timeout rate rises
  • gateway fallback rate rises
  • TTFT increases for one provider only

First response:

  1. Confirm whether the issue is provider-specific or internal.
  2. Check gateway route distribution.
  3. Enable fallback to secondary provider if not already active.
  4. Reduce retries to avoid amplification.
  5. Notify customer-facing teams if degradation is visible.

Safe automation:

if provider_error_rate > threshold for 3 minutes:
  reduce traffic to provider
  route eligible tenants to fallback
  page on-call

Tradeoff:

Fallback may change quality, latency, cost, and compliance posture. Do not fallback healthcare or residency-bound traffic to an unapproved provider.

Post-incident:

  • compare fallback quality
  • calculate extra cost
  • review tenants that could not fail over
  • update provider status detection

GPU Failure Playbook

GPU failures are not always clean crashes. Sometimes the node lives but performance degrades.

Detection:

  • GPU ECC errors
  • worker restarts
  • CUDA out-of-memory errors
  • rising queue time on one replica
  • abnormal TPOT on one node
  • health check failures
  • temperature or power anomalies

First response:

  1. Drain the unhealthy replica.
  2. Stop routing new requests to it.
  3. Let in-flight requests finish if possible.
  4. Replace the node or restart the worker.
  5. Watch capacity and queue depth.

Safe automation:

if worker_health == unhealthy:
  mark replica not ready
  drain traffic
  restart once
  if unhealthy again:
    cordon node
    page on-call

Tradeoff:

Restarting can clear transient failures, but repeated restart loops hide deeper problems. Cordon after repeated failure.

Post-incident:

  • inspect driver/runtime versions
  • check workload that triggered failure
  • review memory pressure and context length
  • add regression load test if a traffic pattern caused it

Cost Spike Playbook

Cost incidents are production incidents.

Detection:

  • tenant spend z-score above threshold
  • output tokens/request jumps
  • agent tool-call count jumps
  • batch job cost exceeds estimate
  • cache hit rate collapses
  • route distribution shifts to expensive model

First response:

  1. Identify tenant, task, route, model, and token layer.
  2. Check whether spike is abuse, bug, launch traffic, or expected growth.
  3. Tighten rate limits if abnormal.
  4. Pause batch or agent workflows if runaway.
  5. Notify owner and customer success if tenant-facing.

Safe automation:

if tenant_spend_z_score > 4:
  pause high-cost routes
  apply stricter token limits
  page on-call

Tradeoff:

Aggressive cost controls can break customer workflows. Use graduated responses: alert, throttle, pause.

Post-incident:

  • add task-specific budget
  • fix prompt/context bloat
  • add cache or batch path
