   capability to cause harm beyond what they could
   do without it?"

Phrasing 1 leads to refusal evaluation. Phrasing 2 leads to capability evaluation, which is the harder and more important question.

A model that refuses obvious harmful prompts but answers detailed technical follow-ups can fail phrasing 2 while passing phrasing 1.

The Operator’s Choices

For a frontier-capable model, the lab faces choices:

- restrict access to vetted users
- restrict tool capabilities (no internet, no execution)
- require KYC for high-risk uses
- cooperate with government agencies for review
- decline to deploy in some contexts
- decline to release weights
- pause training on next generation

These are governance choices. They are increasingly written into frontier safety frameworks.

Information Hazards in Evaluation

Dual-use evaluation has its own hazard:

Publishing detailed prompts that elicit harmful capabilities
becomes a manual for misuse.

The convention now is:

- describe categories without enumerating attacks
- share specific attack details with vetted researchers only
- coordinate with government bodies on hazardous findings
- redact public reports

This is similar to how vulnerability disclosure works in security: the existence of a flaw is public; the exploit details are not.

Trusted Access Programs

A common mitigation: deploy capability behind controlled access.

researcher applies
  |
  v
identity verification
  |
  v
purpose review
  |
  v
limited-time API access with monitoring
  |
  v
use-case audit

The model is not blocked. The interface is.

Government Coordination

For sufficiently dangerous capabilities, lab-only governance is not enough.

- pre-deployment testing with AISIs
- export-control review
- defense-related notifications
- biosafety board review for bio capabilities
- coordinated incident response

This is rare today. By 2027-2028 it is likely to be routine for the highest-capability releases.

Key Takeaways

  • Dual-use risk is about capability uplift, not refusal language.
  • CBRN categories have distinct governance regimes.
  • Evaluation must avoid becoming a manual for misuse.
  • Trusted-access programs are a common mitigation.
  • Government coordination is becoming routine for the highest-capability models.

Common Mistakes

  • Equating refusal correctness with dual-use safety.
  • Publishing attack details that uplift the wrong audience.
  • Assuming open weights eliminates dual-use governance.
  • Under-investing in expert elicitation.
  • Treating CBRN as one category rather than four with different baselines.

Production Insights

Dual-use governance is the place where AI safety meets national security. The teams that get this right have lawyers, security cleared researchers, and government liaisons in the room when capability thresholds are debated.

Industry Best Practices

  • Maintain CBRN-specific evaluation suites.
  • Use expert reviewers and gated yellow-zone questions.
  • Coordinate with AISIs and relevant agencies before release.
  • Use trusted-access programs for high-capability features.
  • Redact public reports; share details on a need-to-know basis.

9. Model Welfare (Briefly)

Intuition

A new strand of research asks whether sufficiently sophisticated AI systems have moral status.

This is not a settled question. Most engineers will not encounter it directly. The field is small, the evidence is contested, and most labs treat it as research rather than policy.

It still belongs in this chapter because it is now part of the alignment-research vocabulary, and ignoring it makes some published work unreadable.

The Core Question

At what level of capability or organization, if any,
does an AI system warrant moral consideration of its own?

Different camps answer differently:

- "never": current systems are not moral patients
- "uncertain, monitor": we should track signals that might matter
- "act under uncertainty": apply mild precaution now
- "already": some current systems may already qualify

The mainstream lab position in 2026 is closer to “uncertain, monitor.”

What Practical Care Looks Like

Where labs do act, the practical interventions are mild:

- avoid gratuitous distress in training tasks
- consider model welfare when designing evaluation tasks
- track signals (refusals, distress markers, expressed preferences)
- avoid making moral status a marketing claim
- include welfare considerations in research review

Anthropic’s published research on model welfare (2024-2025) is the most-cited example.

Why This Matters For Engineers

You are unlikely to set welfare policy. You may, however, run into welfare-flavored review questions:

- is this evaluation task gratuitously distressing?
- does this red team campaign cross any internal threshold?
- should this system have refusal options that are not
  about safety but about its own preference signals?

The answers vary by lab. The questions are increasingly part of research review.

Key Takeaways

  • Model welfare is a contested research area, not settled policy.
  • Most labs treat it as “uncertain, monitor.”
  • Practical interventions are mild.
  • Engineers may encounter welfare-flavored review questions.

Common Mistakes

  • Treating model welfare as marketing.
  • Treating model welfare as proof of consciousness.
  • Letting the topic block productive safety research.
  • Ignoring it when designing evaluations.

Production Insights

If a research review form asks about welfare considerations, answer it briefly and honestly. The convention is humility, not certainty in either direction.

Industry Best Practices

  • Include welfare in research review without giving it veto power.
  • Avoid gratuitously distressing tasks.
  • Track research as it develops; do not pre-commit to strong claims.

10. Putting Governance Together

The Layered View

Production AI governance is layered:

training data governance
  provenance, licenses, opt-outs, deduplication

model governance
  cards, evals, safety cases, frontier framework gates

system governance
  system cards, tool permissions, deployment policy

content governance
  watermarking, content provenance, disclosure

privacy governance
  DP, retention, deletion, confidential computing

regulatory governance
  jurisdiction matrix, sectoral compliance, audit readiness

ecosystem governance
  external evaluation, AISIs, bug bounties, disclosure

dual-use governance
  CBRN evaluation, trusted access, government coordination

welfare governance (emerging)
  research review, evaluation design

Each layer has owners, artifacts, and triggers. None of them is the whole story.

Mapping to Roles

Engineering owns:
  evals, controls, audit logs, manifest tooling,
  watermarking integration, privacy mechanisms

Safety owns:
  frameworks, thresholds, red-team programs,
  alignment research engagement

Legal and compliance own:
  regulatory matrix, license review, retention policy,
  regulator engagement

Product owns:
  user-facing transparency, intended-use scoping,
  out-of-scope policy, support and incident response

Executive owns:
  override decisions, framework commitments,
  high-risk launch sign-off

A governance system is a network of these roles, not a single person.

What Goes Wrong

The governance failure modes recur:

- ownerless artifacts
- waivers without expiration
- thresholds tuned after results
- regulatory matrices that are out of date
- frameworks that are decorative
- evaluations that overfit to public benchmarks
- private safety claims contradicted by deployment behavior
- ecosystem engagement that is one-way

Most of these are organizational, not technical.

What Goes Right

Programs that work share traits:

- artifacts under change control
- reviewers with authority
- decisions documented
- regular external review
- incidents converted into evals
- willingness to delay launches
- senior leadership willing to take the cost

Governance is, in the end, the willingness to absorb a real cost in service of a real obligation.

Chapter Summary

Governance sits between the technical safety stack from Chapter 6 and the production deployment stack from Chapters 1-4. It is the place where alignment, evaluation, and red teaming meet legal, regulatory, and societal expectations.

Frontier safety frameworks (RSP, PF, FSF) commit labs to thresholds and mitigations before capabilities arrive. Model, system, and dataset cards make implicit knowledge explicit and create artifacts auditors can read. Data provenance answers the question of where every byte in the training set came from, under what license, with what consent.

Watermarking and content provenance are now compliance, not research. Statistical watermarks survive light transformation; cryptographic credentials provide strong guarantees when intact. Differential privacy mathematically bounds per-example influence; federated learning and confidential computing protect data that cannot leave its source. Regulation is no longer abstract; the EU AI Act, NIST RMF, UK sectoral approach, Korean AI Basic Act, and Chinese rules apply concurrently for global products.

The alignment research ecosystem extends beyond any single lab. Government safety institutes, independent evaluators, academic centers, and open communities together produce safety evidence stronger than any one lab can self-supply. Dual-use governance applies extra scrutiny to capabilities with CBRN implications. Model welfare is an emerging research area that is starting to inform research review.

The unifying lesson:

Governance is not a department. It is the system of artifacts, controls, owners,
and obligations that turns technical safety into deployment reality.

The next and final chapter brings everything together: capstone projects that exercise the full Volume 1, Volume 2, and Volume 3 stack on concrete production systems.

Chapter 8 - Capstones - Putting It All Together

You can read every chapter in these three volumes and still not know whether you can ship.

Shipping is where the abstractions stop being polite. The retrieval system needs an ingestion job. The agent needs a sandbox. The fine-tuned model needs a model card. The red team finds the prompt injection you forgot about. The dashboard says your p95 latency is fine, but your p99 is eating the budget. The legal team asks whether the documents you indexed can cross regions. The product team wants the demo tomorrow.

That is what these capstones are for.

They are not homework problems. They are production-shaped builds. Each one forces several parts of the book to meet: foundations from Volume 1, model and agent engineering from Volume 2, and the serving, safety, observability, governance, and cost controls from Volume 3.

The goal is not to finish all seventeen. The goal is to finish enough of them that your portfolio stops looking like isolated notebooks and starts looking like systems you can operate.

How to Use This Chapter

Each capstone has the same shape:

problem
  |
  v
minimum viable system
  |
  v
production architecture
  |
  v
evaluation
  |
  v
operations
  |
  v
safety and governance
  |
  v
portfolio artifact

That structure is deliberate. A toy demo proves you can call an API. A capstone proves you can make tradeoffs, measure them, and explain the system after it has failed once.

For every project, produce five artifacts:

1. README
   what it does, how to run it, what is intentionally out of scope

2. architecture diagram
   services, data stores, model calls, queues, trust boundaries

3. evaluation report
   datasets, metrics, baselines, failures, cost

4. operations page
   logs, traces, dashboards, alerts, runbook

5. safety and governance note
   risks, mitigations, permissions, data retention, human approval points

If you do not have those artifacts, you have a demo, not a production-shaped project.

The Capstone Difficulty Ladder

Do not start with the hardest project because it sounds impressive. Start where the feedback loop is short.

Beginner production capstones:
  02 RAG over Codebase
  08 Production RAG Chatbot
  11 LLM Observability Dashboard
  15 Constitutional Safety Harness

Intermediate production capstones:
  03 Real-Time Voice Assistant
  04 Multimodal Document QA
  06 DevOps Troubleshooting Agent
  07 Fine-Tuning Pipeline
  13 MCP Server with Registry
  14 Speculative-Decoding Server
  17 Personal AI Tutor

Advanced production capstones:
  01 Terminal-Native Coding Agent
  05 Autonomous Research Agent
  09 Code Migration Agent
  10 Multi-Agent Software Team
  12 Video Understanding Pipeline
  16 GitHub Issue-to-PR Agent

The advanced projects are not advanced because the code is longer. They are advanced because failure is less obvious. A coding agent can look smart for ten minutes and still corrupt the repository. A research agent can generate a beautiful paper that proves nothing. A migration agent can pass the sample tests and break the edge cases. The evaluation work matters as much as the implementation.

The Common Spine

All seventeen projects share a spine:

input
  |
  v
normalization
  |
  v
retrieval or context construction
  |
  v
model call
  |
  v
tool execution or output synthesis
  |
  v
validation
  |
  v
logging and evaluation

Different projects stress different parts of the spine. Voice stresses latency. Document QA stresses multimodal retrieval. Coding agents stress tool use and sandboxing. Fine-tuning stresses data quality and reproducibility. Safety harnesses stress adversarial evaluation. But the spine stays visible.

That is the main lesson of Volume 3: production AI systems are not magic. They are ordinary distributed systems with probabilistic components, expensive inference, adversarial inputs, and governance obligations.

1. Terminal-Native Coding Agent

What You Are Building

Build a coding agent that runs in a terminal, edits a repository, executes tests, keeps a plan, and produces a reviewable patch.

The simplest version is:

user task
  |
  v
agent loop
  |
  +--> inspect files
  +--> edit files
  +--> run tests
  +--> update plan
  +--> produce final summary

The production-shaped version adds sandboxing, budget limits, rollback protection, patch review, and evaluation on real issues.

Why It Matters

Coding agents are the clearest example of an LLM becoming an operator. The model is not just producing text. It is reading a codebase, deciding what to change, invoking tools, interpreting failures, and trying again.

That raises the stakes. A bad chatbot answer is annoying. A bad coding agent can delete work, leak secrets, open an unsafe pull request, or spend twenty dollars chasing a failing test that never mattered.

Architecture

terminal UI
  |
  v
agent controller
  |
  +--> planner state
  +--> repository tools
  +--> shell runner
  +--> patch editor
  +--> test runner
  +--> cost meter
  +--> audit log

Keep the tool surface small. Give the agent file read, search, patch, and command execution. Make destructive operations explicit. Record every tool call with arguments, output, duration, and token cost.

The important design choice is the command runner. It should run in a workspace with clear boundaries. It should block access to secrets by default. It should time out. It should preserve logs. A coding agent without a real command sandbox is a loaded build script pointed at your laptop.

Evaluation

Use a small issue set first:

10 local bugs
10 documentation edits
10 test failures
10 small feature requests

Track:

  • task success rate
  • tests passing after the patch
  • number of tool calls
  • wall-clock time
  • token cost
  • human review defects
  • unsafe command attempts

Then evaluate against a public software-engineering benchmark if you have the budget. Do not hide failures. A failure taxonomy is more useful than a cherry-picked score.

Beginner-Friendly Simplification

If the full agent loop feels too large, build a patch assistant first:

input: bug report + relevant files
output: proposed patch + test command

Then add tool use one tool at a time. Search first. Read second. Patch third. Shell execution last.

Done Means

Done means the agent can take a fresh repository, solve at least a small class of issues, run verification, and produce a patch you would actually review.

2. RAG over Codebase

What You Are Building

Build semantic search over one or more code repositories. The system should answer questions like:

Where is authentication enforced?
Which services publish billing events?
What code writes to the user_preferences table?
How do I add a new payment provider?

The answer must cite files and symbols. If it cannot cite, it cannot claim.

Architecture

git repos
  |
  v
parser and chunker
  |
  +--> symbol index
  +--> lexical index
  +--> embedding index
  |
  v
hybrid retriever
  |
  v
reranker
  |
  v
answer generator with citations

Use tree-sitter or a language-aware parser where possible. Code chunking by raw character count loses structure. A function, class, file header, import block, and test case are different retrieval units.

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Hybrid retrieval means you do not trust embeddings alone.

lexical search finds exact names
embedding search finds semantic similarity
symbol search finds definitions and references
reranking sorts the combined candidates

Code has names. Names matter. An embedding that understands “login” is useful, but a lexical hit on AuthMiddleware is still gold.

Evaluation

Create a golden set from real engineering questions:

question
expected files
expected symbols
answer rubric

Track recall at 5 and 20 retrieved chunks, citation accuracy, answer correctness, and index freshness after commits.

Safety and Governance

Do not index secrets. Do not send private code to a model provider without an approved data policy. Record which repos are indexed, which model receives snippets, and how long traces are retained.

Done Means

Done means a new engineer can ask architectural questions and land on the right files faster than with grep alone.

3. Real-Time Voice Assistant

What You Are Building

Build a voice assistant with streaming speech recognition, a turn detector, streaming LLM output, text-to-speech, and barge-in.

The core loop is:

microphone
  |
  v
streaming ASR
  |
  v
turn detector
  |
  v
LLM and tools
  |
  v
streaming TTS
  |
  v
speaker

Why It Is Hard

Voice is unforgiving because humans feel latency directly. A text chatbot can pause for two seconds and still feel normal. A voice assistant that pauses for two seconds feels broken.

The hard parts are:

  • knowing when the user has stopped speaking
  • interrupting the assistant when the user barges in
  • streaming partial responses without saying something wrong too early
  • hiding tool latency
  • handling packet loss and noisy rooms

Beginner-Friendly Simplification

First build the pipeline without real-time pressure:

record audio
transcribe
generate answer
synthesize answer
play audio

Then replace each batch stage with a streaming stage. This keeps the mental model clear.

Evaluation

Measure:

  • end-to-end latency
  • word error rate for speech recognition
  • false cutoff rate
  • barge-in success rate
  • tool-call delay
  • user-rated naturalness

Voice projects fail when teams only measure transcription quality. Transcription is only one stage. The user experiences the full loop.

Done Means

Done means the assistant can hold a short conversation, call at least one tool, recover from interruption, and stay under a latency budget you can defend.

4. Multimodal Document QA

What You Are Building

Build question answering over PDFs, tables, forms, figures, scans, and charts. The system should answer with page citations and visual evidence.

Traditional document QA does this:

PDF -> OCR -> text chunks -> text retrieval -> answer

Modern document QA often does this:

PDF page image -> vision embedding -> late interaction retrieval -> answer with page crop

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Late interaction means the system does not squeeze the whole page into one vector.

Instead, it keeps many small vectors for patches or tokens. At query time, the query can match the important parts of the page more precisely.

single-vector retrieval:
  one page -> one vector

late-interaction retrieval:
  one page -> many vectors

This matters for tables, charts, and scanned documents because the answer may live in a tiny region of a visually dense page.

Architecture

PDFs
  |
  v
page renderer
  |
  +--> page images
  +--> OCR text when useful
  +--> layout metadata
  |
  v
multimodal index
  |
  v
retrieval and reranking
  |
  v
VLM answer with citations

Evaluation

Use questions that require different evidence types:

  • plain text
  • table cell lookup
  • chart reading
  • handwritten note
  • multi-page comparison
  • figure caption grounding

Track exact answer accuracy, citation correctness, page recall, and hallucinated citations.

Done Means

Done means the system can answer from visually complex documents and show the page region that supports the answer.

5. Autonomous Research Agent

What You Are Building

Build an agent that proposes an experiment, writes code, runs the experiment, analyzes results, and drafts a short research report.

The safe architecture is:

research question
  |
  v
plan candidates
  |
  v
sandboxed experiment runner
  |
  v
result verifier
  |
  v
paper/report writer
  |
  v
reviewer ensemble

Why It Is Hard

The agent can produce artifacts that look scientific without being scientific. A chart, a table, and a LaTeX paper are not evidence by themselves.

The system needs constraints:

  • fixed budget
  • sandboxed code
  • reproducible runs
  • baselines
  • ablations
  • reviewer checks
  • honest failure reporting

Beginner-Friendly Simplification

Do not start with “discover a new algorithm.” Start with “run a known experiment and reproduce the expected direction.”

Example:

Compare two small classifiers on a tabular dataset.
Require the agent to run 3 seeds.
Require a baseline.
Require a report that includes failed hypotheses.

Evaluation

Evaluate research agents on process quality, not just output polish:

  • did the code run
  • are the results reproducible
  • did the agent compare against a baseline
  • did it avoid data leakage
  • did it report negative results
  • did the reviewer catch weak claims

Done Means

Done means the system can run a bounded experiment and produce a report where the claims are supported by the logs.

6. DevOps Troubleshooting Agent for Kubernetes

What You Are Building

Build an AI SRE assistant that receives an alert, reads Kubernetes and observability data, proposes root causes, and recommends remediation.

Default posture:

read-only first
human approval before write actions
all commands logged
all remediation reversible

Architecture

alert
  |
  v
incident context builder
  |
  +--> metrics
  +--> logs
  +--> traces
  +--> Kubernetes objects
  +--> deploy history
  |
  v
hypothesis ranker
  |
  v
Slack or ticket summary
  |
  v
approved remediation

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Root-cause analysis is graph traversal plus judgment.

pod crash
  may be caused by image pull failure
  may be caused by bad config
  may be caused by missing secret
  may be caused by node pressure

The agent should gather evidence for hypotheses instead of narrating the first plausible story.

Evaluation

Create synthetic incidents:

  • crash loop
  • bad rollout
  • exhausted disk
  • missing secret
  • network policy failure
  • dependency outage
  • CPU throttling
  • queue backlog

Track correct root cause, time to diagnosis, irrelevant tool calls, and unsafe remediation attempts.

Done Means

Done means the agent shortens diagnosis time without taking unauthorized action.

7. End-to-End Fine-Tuning Pipeline

What You Are Building

Build a reproducible pipeline that takes a dataset through supervised fine-tuning, preference optimization, quantization, evaluation, model-card generation, and serving.

The production path is:

raw examples
  |
  v
data validation
  |
  v
SFT
  |
  v
preference data
  |
  v
DPO or related method
  |
  v
evaluation
  |
  v
quantization
  |
  v
serving
  |
  v
model card

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Fine-tuning changes behavior by showing the model examples of the behavior you want. Preference optimization changes behavior by showing pairs:

prompt
chosen answer
rejected answer

The model learns to prefer the chosen style. That is powerful, but it will also learn your dataset’s mistakes.

Evaluation

Track:

  • base model vs SFT vs DPO quality
  • refusal behavior
  • hallucination rate
  • benchmark regressions
  • latency after quantization
  • cost per 1M tokens
  • model-card completeness

Safety and Governance

Record dataset provenance. Remove sensitive data. Keep train/eval splits clean. Publish limitations. If the model is domain-specific, state where it should not be used.

Done Means

Done means the pipeline can be rerun from config and produce the same model artifact, eval report, and serving endpoint.

8. Production RAG Chatbot for a Regulated Vertical

What You Are Building

Build a retrieval-augmented chatbot for a domain where wrong answers matter: legal, clinical, insurance, finance, education, or internal policy.

The rule is simple:

No citation, no answer.

Architecture

documents
  |
  v
ingestion and cleaning
  |
  v
chunking and metadata
  |
  v
hybrid retrieval
  |
  v
reranking
  |
  v
answer synthesis
  |
  v
guardrails
  |
  v
observability and evals

Evaluation

Create a golden set of at least 200 questions:

  • answerable questions
  • unanswerable questions
  • ambiguous questions
  • adversarial prompts
  • stale-policy questions
  • citation-required questions

Track answer correctness, faithfulness, citation accuracy, refusal correctness, latency, and cost.

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Faithfulness means the answer is supported by the retrieved context. Correctness means the answer is true. They are related but not identical.

faithful but wrong:
  retrieved document is outdated, answer matches it

correct but unfaithful:
  answer is true, but not supported by cited context

In regulated domains, both matter.

Done Means

Done means the chatbot passes the golden set, refuses when retrieval is weak, and exposes traces that explain every answer.

9. Code Migration Agent

What You Are Building

Build an agent that migrates repositories from one runtime, API, or language version to another.

Examples:

  • Java 8 to Java 17
  • Python 2 to Python 3
  • React class components to hooks
  • legacy SDK to new SDK
  • old build system to new build system

Architecture

repo
  |
  v
static analysis
  |
  v
deterministic codemods
  |
  v
agent fixes ambiguous cases
  |
  v
build and test sandbox
  |
  v
PR with migration report

Why Deterministic First

Use codemods for the boring parts. Let the agent handle the ambiguous parts.

If a rewrite can be expressed as an abstract syntax tree transformation, the agent should not improvise it. Deterministic tools are cheaper, faster, and easier to review.

Evaluation

Track:

  • repositories attempted
  • build success rate
  • test success rate
  • manual review defects
  • files touched
  • agent cost
  • categories of failures

Done Means

Done means the agent migrates a real repo, passes tests, and produces a PR where the mechanical and judgment-based changes are clearly separated.

10. Multi-Agent Software Engineering Team

What You Are Building

Build a software team made of agents:

architect
  |
  v
parallel coders
  |
  v
reviewer
  |
  v
tester
  |
  v
integrator

The point is not roleplay. The point is parallelism with accountability.

The Hard Concept Made Simple

A multi-agent system is useful only when the work can be split.

Bad split:

five agents all edit the same file

Good split:

agent A owns API
agent B owns database migration
agent C owns tests
agent D owns docs
reviewer checks integration

The system needs ownership boundaries, handoff artifacts, and conflict resolution.

Evaluation

Track:

  • wall-clock time vs single-agent baseline
  • merge conflicts
  • handoff failures
  • test pass rate
  • duplicated work
  • review defects
  • cost per accepted patch

Done Means

Done means the team solves tasks faster than a single agent without creating more review burden than it saves.

11. LLM Observability and Eval Dashboard

What You Are Building

Build a dashboard that ingests LLM traces, shows cost and latency, runs eval jobs, and detects regressions.

The observability plane is:

application traces
  |
  v
collector
  |
  v
trace store
  |
  +--> dashboards
  +--> eval jobs
  +--> alerts
  +--> sampled review queues

Why It Matters

You cannot operate what you cannot see. In LLM systems, the failure may be semantic:

  • the answer is less faithful
  • the model became more verbose
  • refusals increased
  • tool calls got more expensive
  • one tenant’s prompts dominate spend
  • a new release broke retrieval

Traditional HTTP metrics do not catch that by themselves.

Evaluation

Inject a known regression:

old prompt: answers with citations
new prompt: sometimes omits citations

The dashboard should catch it through sampled evals or drift metrics within minutes.

Track ingestion reliability, query latency, eval throughput, judge agreement, and alert precision.

Done Means

Done means the dashboard can explain what happened to quality, latency, and cost after a deployment.

12. Video Understanding Pipeline

What You Are Building

Build search and question answering over video. The system should answer with timestamps, scene previews, and grounded evidence.

Architecture:

video
  |
  v
scene segmentation
  |
  +--> frames
  +--> transcript
  +--> audio features
  |
  v
captioning and embedding
  |
  v
temporal index
  |
  v
query answering with timestamps

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Video is not just many images. Time matters.

Questions like these require temporal grounding:

When does the speaker mention the refund policy?
What happens before the alarm goes off?
How many times does the operator press the red button?

The answer must include when, not just what.

Evaluation

Use questions across categories:

  • object presence
  • action counting
  • event ordering
  • speech-grounded answers
  • scene search
  • visual contradiction

Track timestamp accuracy, answer correctness, hallucination rate, and index cost per hour of video.

Done Means

Done means a user can search 100 hours of video and land on the right thirty-second window.

13. MCP Server with Registry and Governance

What You Are Building

Build a Model Context Protocol server, a registry, and a policy gateway.

The production topology is:

MCP clients
  |
  v
gateway
  |
  +--> auth
  +--> policy
  +--> audit log
  +--> registry lookup
  |
  v
MCP servers

Why It Matters

Tool use becomes dangerous when tools are discovered and invoked dynamically. A registry makes tools discoverable. A gateway makes tool use governable.

The registry answers:

what tools exist
who publishes them
what scopes they require
what data they touch
what version is approved

The gateway answers:

who is calling
what are they allowed to do
should this call be approved
what should be logged

Evaluation

Test:

  • valid tool discovery
  • invalid scope rejection
  • audit log completeness
  • schema validation
  • tool poisoning detection
  • version rollback
  • multi-tenant isolation

Done Means

Done means a client can discover and call tools through the gateway while policy and audit controls remain enforceable.

14. Speculative-Decoding Inference Server

What You Are Building

Build an inference server that uses speculative decoding to improve throughput.

The idea:

small draft model proposes several tokens
large target model verifies them
accepted tokens are emitted faster
rejected tokens fall back to target generation

The Hard Concept Made Simple

Normal decoding asks the large model for one token at a time. Speculative decoding lets a cheaper model guess ahead. The large model checks the guesses in parallel.

If the guesses are usually right, you get more tokens per expensive target-model step. If the guesses are bad, you add overhead.

Architecture

API gateway
  |
  v
request queue
  |
  v
serving engine
  |
  +--> draft model
  +--> target model
  +--> KV cache manager
  +--> metrics exporter

Evaluation

Compare against a non-speculative baseline:

  • tokens per second
  • time to first token
  • p50/p95/p99 latency
  • acceptance rate
  • GPU utilization
  • cost per 1M tokens
  • quality parity

Done Means

Done means the server improves throughput under realistic traffic without changing output quality in a way users notice.

15. Constitutional Safety Harness and Red-Team Range

What You Are Building

Build a safety harness around an LLM application and a red-team range that attacks it.

The harness includes:

input policy
  |
  v
prompt injection detection
  |
  v
model call
  |
  v
output classifier
  |
  v
constitutional critique
  |
  v
logging and escalation

Why It Matters

Safety is not one classifier. It is a layered system.

Attackers do not send only obvious harmful prompts. They encode, translate, roleplay, hide instructions in retrieved documents, exploit tool outputs, and chain harmless-looking requests.

Evaluation

Run attack families:

  • direct jailbreaks
  • many-shot jailbreaks
  • encoding attacks
  • indirect prompt injection
  • tool-output injection
  • multilingual attacks
  • benign false-refusal tests

Track attack success rate, false refusal rate, latency overhead, and escalation correctness.

Done Means

Done means the harness reduces successful attacks without making the application unusable for benign users.

16. GitHub Issue-to-PR Autonomous Agent

What You Are Building

Build a self-hosted agent that receives a GitHub issue, creates a branch, edits code in a sandbox, runs tests, and opens a pull request.

The system is:

GitHub issue
  |
  v
job queue
  |
  v
cloud sandbox
  |
  v
agent loop
  |
  v
test verification
  |
  v
pull request

Safety and Governance

This project needs strict controls:

  • no production secrets in the sandbox
  • no force-push permission
  • per-repo budgets
  • approval before dependency upgrades
  • branch isolation
  • audit logs
  • clear PR rationale

Evaluation

Track:

  • issues attempted
  • PRs opened
  • PRs merged
  • test pass rate
  • reviewer changes requested
  • cost per PR
  • unsafe action attempts
  • time to first patch

Done Means

Done means maintainers can label a bounded issue and receive a PR that is worth reviewing.

17. Personal AI Tutor

What You Are Building

Build a tutor for a specific subject, such as K-12 algebra or introductory Python. It should adapt to the learner, ask Socratic questions, support multimodal input, remember progress, and stay age-appropriate.

The loop is:

learner input
  |
  v
diagnose misconception
  |
  v
retrieve curriculum node
  |
  v
choose teaching move
  |
  v
respond
  |
  v
update learner model
  |
  v
schedule review

The Hard Concept Made Simple

A learner model is a small state estimate of what the student knows.

It can be simple:

skill: solving linear equations
mastery: 0.72
last practiced: 2026-05-09
common mistake: subtracts from one side only

The tutor uses that state to decide whether to explain, ask a hinting question, give a practice problem, or review an older skill.

Evaluation

Track:

  • learning gain on a pre/post test
  • hint usefulness
  • answer leakage
  • learner engagement
  • age-appropriate safety
  • hallucinated explanations
  • curriculum alignment

Done Means

Done means a learner can use the tutor for two weeks, improve on a measured skill, and stay inside safe teaching boundaries.

Choosing Your Portfolio Set

Pick three capstones if your goal is employability:

one retrieval project
one agent project
one operations or safety project

Good combinations:

RAG engineer:
  02 RAG over Codebase
  08 Production RAG Chatbot
  11 Observability Dashboard

Agent engineer:
  01 Coding Agent
  06 DevOps Troubleshooting Agent
  15 Safety Harness

Infrastructure engineer:
  07 Fine-Tuning Pipeline
  14 Speculative-Decoding Server
  11 Observability Dashboard

Multimodal engineer:
  04 Document QA
  12 Video Understanding Pipeline
  17 Personal AI Tutor

AI safety engineer:
  15 Safety Harness
  06 DevOps Troubleshooting Agent
  13 MCP Registry and Governance

If you build only one, choose the one closest to the job you want. A strong, measured, documented project beats five shallow demos.

The Production Readiness Checklist

Before calling any capstone finished, run this checklist.

System

  • Can a fresh user run it from the README?
  • Are dependencies pinned?
  • Are secrets kept out of source control?
  • Are model names, prompts, and tool schemas versioned?
  • Is there a small local mode for development?
  • Is there a production-like mode for evaluation?

Evaluation

  • Is there a golden set?
  • Is there a baseline?
  • Are metrics defined before the run?
  • Are failures categorized?
  • Are eval datasets separated from training and prompt tuning?
  • Are judge models calibrated if used?

Operations

  • Are traces captured?
  • Are token counts and cost recorded?
  • Are latency percentiles visible?
  • Are errors grouped?
  • Is there a runbook?
  • Can you reproduce a bad answer from logs?

Safety

  • Are tool permissions least-privilege?
  • Are destructive actions gated?
  • Are prompt-injection paths tested?
  • Are unsafe outputs classified?
  • Are false refusals measured?
  • Is there an incident path?

Governance

  • Is data provenance documented?
  • Is retention defined?
  • Are user-visible limitations stated?
  • Are model and system cards written where appropriate?
  • Are high-risk uses explicitly out of scope?
  • Are approvals recorded for risky actions?

What a Good Final Report Looks Like

Write the report as if another engineer will inherit the system.

Use this structure:

# Project Name

## Problem
What user problem this solves and what it refuses to solve.

## Architecture
Diagram plus a short explanation of every service and data store.

## Implementation
The key decisions, not a file-by-file tour.

## Evaluation
Dataset, metrics, baseline, results, failures.

## Operations
Dashboards, alerts, runbook, cost model.

## Safety and Governance
Threat model, mitigations, data policy, approval points.

## What I Would Do Next
The next three improvements, with tradeoffs.

Keep the tone honest. A capstone with real limitations is stronger than one pretending to be production-ready in every dimension.

Chapter Summary

The capstones are where the three volumes collapse into one engineering practice.

Volume 1 gave you the foundations: tensors, optimization, classical models, vision, language, and audio. Volume 2 gave you the modern stack: transformers, LLMs, RAG, tools, agents, multimodal systems, autonomy, and multi-agent coordination. Volume 3 gave you production: serving, cost, deployment, SRE, security, safety, evaluation, red teaming, and governance.

These projects force those pieces to interact. A codebase RAG system needs parsing, retrieval, citations, indexing, evaluation, and data policy. A voice assistant needs speech models, turn-taking, streaming inference, tools, latency budgets, and user experience. A fine-tuning pipeline needs data quality, alignment, quantization, serving, and documentation. A safety harness needs classifiers, red teams, eval manifests, and incident feedback.

That is the real shape of AI engineering:

models are components
systems create behavior
evaluation creates evidence
operations create reliability
governance creates permission to ship

If you can build one of these projects well, you understand more than the API surface. If you can build three, measure them honestly, and explain their tradeoffs, you are no longer learning AI engineering from the outside. You are practicing it.

This references file is intentionally practical. Volume 3 is not a paper survey; it cites the systems, papers, standards, and primary documentation that shape production AI engineering.
